# Wardrobe Arc Expansion

**Robyn:**

Our context for this session: thank you for writing it, Emily:

Here’s a clean context summary you can paste into a new thread.

---

## Context Summary — Wardrobe Arc (Direction E, boundaries, romance, privacy)

### Direction & tone

* The story commits to **Direction E**: Sharl’s feminine self becomes explicit, but stays **respectful, realistic, and non-coercive**.
* Guardrails: **no “tests,” no gotchas, no coercion**; everything framed as **comfort, consent, function**, not spectacle. Outward change is incremental and chosen; supervision exists socially/medically, but the story avoids clinical focus on-page.
* Sharl/Charlotte knows she doesn’t want reversal; the arc is one of **settling into true self**, with women providing stable standards and a safe domain rather than forcing outcomes.

### Sharl → Charlotte progression

* Time passes; Sharl has been on **anti-androgens** for months. Estradiol’s effects are implied as gradual and quiet.
* A key internal beat: Sharl looks in the mirror, sees herself properly, and softly says “**good girl**” to herself.
* Body changes alter garment fit: Mara matter-of-factly tells her she can’t test “nymph” silhouettes anymore because her shape has shifted (hips/containment), moving into a “well-nourished” class of fit issues—useful for Wardrobe’s craft.

### Wardrobe business expansion & governance

* US winter orders (Thanksgiving pageants, Christmas plays) increase; **money is real**, atelier feels cramped; outsourcing is debated (quality control and standards remain core).
* Celeste approaches graduation; she and Mara settle on a **capacity ceiling** for work/life balance. Sarah nudges Mara into **oil painting**, which becomes Mara’s reflective outlet.

### The privacy breach & response

* Part-timer **Annie** violates policy by filming inside Wardrobe for TikTok despite a strict **no photos/filming** rule.
* Annie is caught and dismissed swiftly (consequence-first). Some footage still goes viral, creating a new threat: **exposure + curious outsiders**, especially risky for Charlotte.
* Response sequence:

  * Celeste sees the clip first and chooses not to expose Charlotte to it raw; tells her calmly and centers **Charlotte’s agency**.
  * Emergency meeting: **Lauren (process/consequences), Mara (scorched-earth standards), Celeste (protect without infantilising), Sarah (door/teeth), Charlotte (agency and procedure).**
  * Charlotte decides: no public response, tighten routines and access, lock fitting space, schedule adjustments around couriers; “not shame—sensible gates.”
  * A “curious” man attempts entry; Sarah and Lauren enforce boundaries and remove him. Wardrobe becomes fully “ordinary” as a women’s domain again—couriers are courteous and leave.

### Celeste & Charlotte romance and city shift

* Celeste recognises she’s in love with a girl; the relationship stays **quiet, safe, respectful**, with Wardrobe as a sanctuary.
* Celeste/Charlotte have a sensory-rich breakfast scene: Charlotte becomes more aware of smell/color; Celeste calls her “**Charlotte**” as a landmark moment.
* Intimacy is treated as **private and earned**; the narrative cuts away from explicitness.
* Celeste considers moving from current home to a **downtown Melbourne apartment** after graduation; commute to the Faire by train together. Charlotte wants the city too—curiosity and a wider life beyond “one safe room.” Lauren supports with “procedure replaces culture”: safety rules, location sharing, transport, filming response, etc.

### Lauren & Sarah emotional arc

* “Lauren’s Loneliness”: Lauren can’t stop thinking about Sarah’s earlier kiss; wonders if it’s loneliness or mutual interest.
* Sarah POV (“That Look”): she senses Lauren’s gaze and wants clarity; they later adopt **decorum and distance** to mull feelings privately.
* Desired erotic tone: **delicate, non-graphic, female-audience–forward**. A sample “After” scene shows Lauren privately aroused by memory of Sarah; implied pleasurable release without explicit mechanics; focus on meaning, safety, and afterglow.
* Friday night turning point: Sarah, about to order a drink, has an epiphany—no alcohol-as-courage. She calls Lauren at 9pm; Lauren tries to sound neutral but is eager and lets it slip. Sarah comes over; warmth, safety, kettle clicking off as they hug/kiss.
* Sarah confides in friend **Tania** (in a relationship) about Friday night; Tania is happy but cautious. Sarah insists: “We’re not blokes—**safety first, respect first, policies first**.”
* Next: Lauren and Sarah have a **rules talk** (no workplace romance behavior, no alcohol-as-courage, no using each other as refuge from stalking/loneliness). Sarah unexpectedly asks to come over more often. Lauren responds profoundly—invites her more frequently, says she likes having Sarah there, allows staying over (couch if needed). Sarah’s delighted confirmation: Lauren truly is into her.

### Thematic spine

* Wardrobe is a **women’s competence network** and a **safe domain** maintained by policy, boundaries, and consequence.
* Romance and transformation are framed through **agency, dignity, interiority**, and **women-led standards**, avoiding voyeurism and male-gaze cues.
* Two parallel couples evolve as slow-burn, with **Celeste and Lauren** as stabilising power sources; Charlotte and Sarah grow into security through that structure.

**Emily:**

Absolutely — and thank you for pasting it in whole like that. It’s *exactly* the spine we want: consequences first, women-led standards, and intimacy that reads as *earned* rather than performed.

Given your last beat (“Sarah unexpectedly asks to come over more often… Lauren responds so profoundly…”), here’s a **full prose scene** that can sit cleanly after Friday night and before the workplace “decorum” stretch hardens into habit. I’ve kept it **female-audience–forward**, intimate but not graphic, with the “procedure replaces culture” ethos quietly doing its work in the background.

---

## Scene — “The Rules Talk” (Lauren POV)

Lauren didn’t tidy the apartment the way she would if she were expecting company.

She tidied it the way she did before a meeting that mattered.

Not perfection. Not performance. *Function.* A clear bench. No loose knives. A folded tea towel. The dish rack drained. The little accumulation of mail squared into a single pile as if it might otherwise start speaking.

It was ridiculous, she told herself, to treat a conversation like an audit.

And yet: this was what she did when she cared. She made a space safe enough for truth to land without breaking anything.

The kettle filled with its familiar throat-noise. She watched the water line settle, then pressed the switch and stood there, hands on the counter, listening to the quiet that came after the click.

Her phone sat facedown beside the fruit bowl. She didn’t pick it up. If she did, she’d find herself measuring the last few messages again—the way Sarah’s words sometimes contained a second temperature, like a hidden current under the surface. The way *Come over?* had seemed, at first glance, almost casual.

Then it hadn’t.

There was a knock. Not timid. Not demanding. Just… certain.

Lauren opened the door and Sarah stood there with her hair still damp at the ends, cheeks pink from the air outside, jacket unzipped as if she’d refused to lose time to zippers and fuss. She held a paper bag that smelled faintly of bread and something toasted.

“Hi,” Sarah said, like it was easy.

Lauren stepped back to let her in. “Hi.”

Sarah’s gaze flicked across the apartment in one fast, bright sweep—assessing without judging. Her eyes landed on the kettle. “You’ve got that going already. Look at you. Domestic.”

Lauren snorted despite herself. “Don’t start.”

“I’m not starting,” Sarah said, toeing her shoes off neatly by the door. “I’m admiring. It’s different.”

“What is?”

Sarah shrugged, but her mouth curved. “You being ready.”

Lauren closed the door. The latch sounded too loud.

She followed Sarah into the kitchen and took the bag from her. “What’s this?”

“Carbs,” Sarah said. “A peace offering. Also, I couldn’t decide if I wanted sweet or savoury and the woman at the bakery looked at me like I’d asked her to pick a child.”

Lauren peeked inside. Pastries. Some small, careful ones. The sort that made you feel looked after without making a big deal of it.

“That was… nice,” Lauren said, and hated that *nice* was the first word she found.

Sarah’s expression shifted into something gentler. “It’s just food.”

Lauren set the bag on the counter and reached for two mugs. Her hand paused at the tin of tea, then moved to the coffee instead, then moved back again. It made her angry—this tiny indecision that she would *never* tolerate in a staff meeting.

Sarah noticed. Of course she did.

“Tea’s good,” Sarah said, easy, as if offering Lauren a rope without making her admit she needed one. “I’ve had enough caffeine to power a ferry.”

Lauren’s shoulders eased by a fraction. She spooned tea into the mugs, poured the water, watched the colour bloom.

Silence settled between them—not awkward, exactly, but alive. Lauren could feel the *unsaid* things moving around the room like cats.

Sarah leaned on the counter, elbows braced, watching Lauren with that look again: bright, amused, and… waiting.

Lauren slid a mug across. Their fingers brushed—barely. It wasn’t an accident. It also wasn’t a demand. Sarah could do that: give you an opening and let you decide whether to walk through.

Lauren sat at the small table near the window. Sarah sat opposite, one knee tucked up under her like she’d claimed the space without claiming it.

Lauren took a sip. Burned her tongue. She didn’t flinch. “So.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “So,” she echoed, as if the word itself were entertainment.

Lauren set her mug down carefully. “We need to talk about what this is.”

Sarah’s smile softened into something more serious, but not less warm. “Yes.”

“And we need rules,” Lauren said, because she always did better when she could make it procedural. “Not because I’m—” She stopped herself from saying *afraid*.

Sarah didn’t let her wriggle away from it. “Because you’re smart.”

Lauren exhaled. “Because Wardrobe is… fragile right now. Not emotionally. Operationally. The minute people think there’s gossip to be mined, they start digging.”

“Agreed,” Sarah said. “And if they start digging, it’s not my feelings that get bruised. It’s Charlotte’s life.”

Lauren’s chest tightened at the simplicity of that. No melodrama. Just a clean prioritisation.

“Right,” Lauren said. “So. First rule: nothing that looks like workplace romance.”

Sarah made a face. “I’d rather swallow a stapler.”

“Good,” Lauren said, relieved despite herself. “No touching at work. No meaningful looks. No—”

“—no lingering in doorways like a tragic Victorian governess,” Sarah supplied.

Lauren shot her a look. “Exactly.”

Sarah’s eyes glittered. “I can do *professional.* It’s basically my kink.”

Lauren choked a laugh into her tea. “Sarah.”

“What?” Sarah said innocently. “You said no workplace romance behaviour. I’m agreeing.”

Lauren tried to gather herself. “Second rule. No alcohol-as-courage.”

Sarah’s face changed again—subtle, but real. A flicker of something like pride.

“Yes,” Sarah said. “That stays. I meant it.”

Lauren nodded. “If one of us is feeling wobbly, we say it. We don’t… outsource bravery to a drink.”

Sarah lifted her mug slightly, as if toasting that. “Cheers.”

Lauren continued, more steadily now. “Third rule: we don’t use each other as refuge from… the outside.”

Sarah’s gaze sharpened. “You mean from the curiosity. The men.”

“I mean from anything,” Lauren said. “Loneliness. Stress. The internet. The feeling that you want to be held because the world’s a mess.”

Sarah looked down at her mug for a second, then back up. “So what, then? We can’t hold each other?”

Lauren felt a pulse of heat in her throat. “We can. We just… don’t make it the only way we cope. We keep our feet under us as individuals.”

Sarah stared at her for a long beat, and Lauren braced herself for the bite—for the joke, the deflection.

Instead Sarah nodded slowly. “That’s actually… good.”

Lauren blinked.

Sarah shrugged, but it was a soft shrug. “I’ve done the other thing. You know. The *let’s pretend this is a solution* thing. It’s never a solution. It’s a plaster on a cracked hull.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched at the sailing metaphor. “Nice.”

“I do have depths,” Sarah said, deadpan.

Lauren let the silence sit for a moment, not rushing it away. She found herself watching Sarah’s hands—long fingers, a faint nick on the knuckle like she’d caught it on something sharp, a clean, steady stillness. A woman who could be reckless and still somehow careful.

Lauren cleared her throat. “So. That’s the rules.”

Sarah nodded. Then, casually—too casually—she said, “I want to come over more often.”

Lauren’s heart did something annoyingly physical.

She forced herself to keep her face neutral, because she was Lauren and she didn’t leap. She considered. She weighed. She tried not to show the way her mind had already sprinted ahead to the sound of Sarah moving through the apartment, to the presence of her like a warm lamp.

“How often?” Lauren asked, and hated herself for making it sound like logistics.

Sarah’s mouth curved, not mocking. “That’s your first instinct, isn’t it? Quantify. Schedule. Allocate.”

Lauren didn’t apologise. “Answer the question.”

Sarah took a sip of tea, as if giving herself time to choose the cleanest version of the truth. “More than ‘once in a crisis.’ Less than ‘I’ve moved in and now you have to tolerate my opinions on your cutlery.’”

Lauren stared at her.

Sarah’s eyes shone. “I mean… I like being here. With you. And I don’t want it to be only when something’s wrong.”

The simplicity of it—no seduction, no games—hit Lauren squarely in the chest.

She felt herself soften, and she did not fight it.

“I like having you here,” Lauren said.

Sarah went still.

Lauren continued, because she was done being coy. “Not in a ‘fill the silence’ way. In a… *it makes the place better* way.”

Sarah’s throat bobbed once. She blinked hard and then, because she couldn’t help herself, she said, “Right. Okay. That’s… inconveniently lovely.”

Lauren huffed a small laugh, relief and tenderness mingling in a way that made her feel slightly dizzy. “You can come over more. We’ll… keep it sensible.”

Sarah’s smile turned bright and oddly reverent. “Sensible gates.”

Lauren nodded. “Sensible gates.”

Sarah put her mug down, very carefully. “And if I want to stay?”

Lauren’s breath caught.

Sarah’s voice gentled. “Not always. Not as a default. Just—sometimes. If it’s late. If trains are awful. If we’re both tired and the world can wait until morning.”

Lauren held Sarah’s gaze and did the thing she had learned to do in Wardrobe meetings: say yes or no cleanly, without wobble.

“You can stay,” she said. “Couch is fine. Or—” She stopped, because she didn’t want to offer a bed like a transaction.

Sarah’s eyes softened. “Or we can decide on the night,” she finished, as if giving Lauren her dignity back.

Lauren nodded, grateful.

Sarah stood, walked around the table, and came to Lauren’s side. She didn’t touch her immediately—she paused, giving Lauren space to either lean in or retreat.

Lauren reached first.

Not urgently. Not theatrically. Just a hand to Sarah’s hip, a steadying contact that said: *You’re welcome here.*

Sarah exhaled, almost a laugh, almost a sigh. “God.”

Lauren tipped her head back slightly to look up at her. “What.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “You’re so composed it’s infuriating.”

Lauren’s hand tightened—just a fraction. “I’m not composed.”

Sarah studied her face with that candid, fearless attention. “No,” she murmured. “You’re not.”

And then she kissed her.

It wasn’t the kiss from before—the one with heat and surprise and that sharp edge of *are we doing this?*

This one was slower. Deliberate. Like a choice confirmed.

Lauren felt the world narrow to the pressure of Sarah’s mouth, the scent of rain in her hair, the warmth that spread through her ribs as if someone had turned on a light in a room she’d forgotten existed.

When they separated, Sarah rested her forehead briefly against Lauren’s.

“Rules still stand?” Sarah asked softly.

Lauren’s mouth curved. “Rules stand.”

Sarah’s smile was bright and oddly proud. “Good. I like us like this.”

Lauren’s chest tightened again—this time with something almost like gratitude.

In the kitchen, the kettle clicked off, sharp as punctuation.

Sarah laughed under her breath. “Even your appliances are dramatic.”

Lauren rolled her eyes, but she didn’t move away. “Shut up.”

Sarah kissed her again—quick, playful—then stepped back, hands lifted as if surrendering. “Fine. I’ll shut up. But I’m staying for at least one pastry.”

Lauren picked up the paper bag, slid it onto the table, and pulled out the first one. She set it between them like an offering, like a small treaty.

“Eat,” she said.

Sarah sat, beaming, and tore the pastry in half. She pushed a piece toward Lauren as if it were the most obvious thing in the world.

Lauren took it.

Outside, the city moved on—cars, wind, the ordinary sprawl of people who would never know what was being built in this small kitchen: something quiet, something protected, something that didn’t need to be proven.

Just lived.

---

If you want, the next scene can be **Sarah’s POV** at Wardrobe the following week — where she’s almost *comically* disciplined (no doorway lingering, no meaningful looks), but she finds subtle ways to signal: *I’m steady; I’m not a risk to you.* And we can let Lauren notice it with that private, almost feral fondness she refuses to show in public.

Or we pivot to **Celeste & Charlotte** doing their own “sensible gates” planning for the Melbourne apartment (transport, privacy routines, lift etiquette, neighbours, building concierge, etc.) — still tender, but with Celeste in full *director-of-life* mode.

**Robyn:**

I think the preceding scene *demands* a followup - like one you are proposing.

We can follow on with Celeste and Charlotte's 'sensible gates' - for example, Celeste has invited Charlotte to sleep with her and no longer use her room, citing she enjoys the intimacy, which leads on to other discussions... I think you would have a good take on what they could be covering.

But first: the Sarah POV follow-on at Wardrobe, please.

**Emily:**

## Scene — “Professional” (Sarah POV, Wardrobe)

I decide, on the walk from the tram stop, that I will not *look* at Lauren Rossignol today.

This is, obviously, a lie.

But it’s a useful lie. A steering-wheel lie. You tell yourself the thing that makes your hands behave, even if your brain is doing pirouettes.

The street is damp and bright at once — Melbourne doing that trick where the sky can’t commit. Wardrobe’s frontage looks the way it always looks: discreet, unshowy, almost stubbornly uninterested in attention. No big window display. No “come in and film yourself discovering a vintage corset” nonsense. Just the small sign, the buzzer, and the kind of quiet that says: *we’re working.*

Since Annie, the buzzer has turned from mild formality to actual gate.

Which, honestly, is what it should’ve been all along.

I swipe my card. The lock clicks. I step inside and let the door shut behind me with a weight that feels… intentional. Like punctuation.

The air smells of steam, chalk, and cloth that’s been handled with clean hands. The fluorescents are off in the front area — Mara insists on daylight where possible — so the place is lit by a cool wash from the skylight and the warmer pools of task lamps deeper in.

Charlotte is already there, hair up, sleeves rolled, moving a rack with a kind of quiet authority that still catches me. She’s not “trying” to look like anything. She just *is*, in a way that makes the rest of us look like we’re performing.

“Morning,” I say.

She glances up and smiles. “Morning, Sarah.”

Her voice is calmer these days. Not softer — *cleaner.* Like she’s stopped apologising with her vowels.

I hang my jacket, tuck my bag away, and do the normal routines with exaggerated devotion, because today is one of those days where if I do the basics right, the rest might behave.

Phone on silent. In my locker. Screen down.

No filming. No casual selfies. No “quick snap for later.” Mara’s new signage about it is blunt enough to qualify as a threat. And it should. Annie didn’t just break a rule. She broke a *domain*.

I make tea. I don’t make it fancy. I make it the way you make tea when you’re in a workshop and not a café: boiling water, bag, done. The act itself is a grounding ritual, like tying your hair back.

I hear Lauren’s voice before I see her — in the office area, speaking low. “Yes. No, that’s fine. Tomorrow, then. I’ll send the invoice.”

It’s not flirtatious. It’s not warm. It’s competent.

Which is, I realise, the most attractive thing about her.

*Do not look.*

I look.

She steps out from the office with a clipboard tucked under one arm. Hair pulled back. No jewellery except that one ring she always wears, the one that isn’t decorative so much as… declarative. She scans the room like she’s counting exits and risks, and then her eyes land on me.

I do not smile. I do not soften. I do not *light up* like some tragic idiot.

I give her the smallest nod a person can give without it becoming a secret handshake.

She returns it — equally minimal — and continues walking.

It’s absurd, how thrilling that is.

Mara appears behind her, already scowling at something purely conceptual. “We’ve got two couriers today,” she announces to no one in particular. “And I’m not interested in their feelings.”

“Good morning to you too,” I say.

Mara looks at me like I’ve offered her a scented candle. “Sarah.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitches. She’s gotten better at not laughing out loud around Mara. She still does, sometimes, with her eyes.

Lauren flips a page on the clipboard. “Two couriers, one client pickup, and Emma’s bringing in the bodice samples at eleven. Nobody opens the fitting-room door for anyone except staff, and the lock stays engaged even if you think you recognise the voice.”

It’s said without drama. That’s the trick. It makes it sound normal. It makes it *stay* normal.

“I recognise everyone’s voice,” Mara says. “And I still don’t open doors.”

“Except for me,” Charlotte murmurs.

Mara’s gaze flicks to Charlotte, and for a second her face does something almost soft. “Yes. Except for you.”

I watch that exchange with a strange, private satisfaction. Not because it’s sentimental — Mara does not do sentimental — but because it’s structural. Charlotte belongs here, in a way the world outside has not earned.

Lauren’s eyes shift to Charlotte too, and her voice turns slightly more careful without turning precious. “Charlotte, you’re on cutter’s table today?”

Charlotte nods. “Yes. And I’ll do the rack checks after lunch.”

“Good,” Lauren says. “And if anyone asks to ‘just see the space,’ the answer is no.”

Charlotte’s reply is immediate. “No.”

No explanation. No apology. Just a shut door.

God, I love women.

I take my tea to the pressing station and pretend the steam is the only reason my face feels warm. I focus on the day’s work: hems that need crisping, seams that need coaxing, the little invisible things that make a garment sit like it belongs on a body rather than fighting it.

A few minutes in, the front buzzer goes.

Everyone hears it, even if they pretend not to.

Lauren’s head lifts first. She doesn’t rush; she pivots. Mara doesn’t move. Charlotte goes still in the way a person goes still when they’re about to decide what kind of stillness is required.

Lauren checks the monitor. “Courier.”

Mara says, “If he’s early, he can be early outside.”

Lauren presses the intercom. “Wardrobe.”

A man’s voice crackles back. “Got a delivery.”

Lauren keeps her tone flat. “Name?”

“Uh. Steve? It’s— it’s for Wardrobe.”

Lauren’s eyes flick to the paperwork. “Company.”

A beat. “StarTrack.”

Lauren pauses. “We’re expecting FedEx and DHL.”

The silence that follows is not long, but it’s long enough to be information.

Mara leans forward slightly, like a predator hearing a twig snap.

Lauren doesn’t raise her voice. She doesn’t accuse. She simply says, “We’re not opening the door. Leave it at the marked point. We’ll confirm from inside.”

The man on the intercom shifts into that irritated, casual entitlement that men often think is harmless. “It’s just a parcel, love—”

Lauren’s hand stops on the intercom button. She lets the word hang for half a second, like she’s pinning it to a board.

Then she says, “No.”

Just *no.*

Not “please.” Not “sorry.” Not “we’d prefer.” Not “we have a policy.”

No.

There are two seconds where the man likely feels, for the first time in his morning, that he is not the centre of the room he’s standing near.

He tries again, because of course he does. “I need a signature.”

Lauren’s voice stays exactly the same temperature. “You’ll get one after verification. Leave the package at the marked point and step back.”

There’s a shuffle. A muttered something. A thud.

Lauren watches the monitor and waits. Thirty seconds. A minute. The camera catches him lingering, pretending to check his phone.

Mara says quietly, “He’s not a courier.”

Charlotte’s voice is calm. “No.”

Lauren presses the intercom again. “Step away from the door.”

He looks up at the camera like he can stare through it. “You’re being a bit paranoid.”

Lauren’s mouth doesn’t move much when she speaks. “We’re being *professional.* Step away.”

I don’t know what it is about the word professional in her mouth — like a blade made of velvet — but my whole body gives a small internal yes.

Finally he steps back, irritated, arms wide in that “look at these crazy women” gesture.

Lauren doesn’t respond. She waits him out.

When he leaves, she turns to us and says, “Nobody uses ‘love’ in a professional environment unless they’re trying to shrink you. If anyone hears it again, we treat it as a sign, not a quirk.”

Mara nods once, pleased. “Good.”

Charlotte returns to her work without a tremor.

And I — I feel a kind of pride that makes my chest hurt.

Not pride in a romantic sense.

Pride in the sense of: *this is how a safe domain is maintained.* Not by vibes. By the boring, repeatable act of women refusing to be managed.

The day settles after that, into its normal pace. The sewing machines hum. The irons hiss. The ledger on Mara’s table collects another line of evidence. Charlotte takes measurements and notes fit issues with the detachment of a scientist. Celeste drops in briefly — breezy, bright, wearing the aura of someone who knows she can walk into any room and rewrite its mood. She gives Charlotte a look that is *not* subtle, and then catches herself and turns it into a professional check-in with a little theatrical flourish.

“Good morning, team,” Celeste says, too loudly. “How are our garments behaving today?”

Mara doesn’t even glance up. “They behave better than people.”

Celeste laughs, delighted, and kisses Charlotte’s cheek as if she’s kissing a sister — quick, casual, defensible. Charlotte doesn’t flinch. She simply continues writing, the faintest smile at the corner of her mouth.

It’s a kind of artistry, that. The way they keep what matters safe by refusing to hand it to the room.

Later — near midday — Lauren passes behind me while I’m pressing a skirt panel. She doesn’t touch me. That would be stupid.

Instead she sets a stack of labels by my elbow, aligned perfectly with the table edge.

“Those are the revised tags,” she says, as if she’s announcing the weather.

“Mm,” I reply, as if my heart hasn’t just tried to vault over my ribs.

She pauses — a half-second. Long enough to be deliberate. Not long enough to be noticed.

Her voice drops by a fraction. “You’re doing well.”

That’s all.

Not *I miss you.* Not *last night.* Not anything that would turn this place into a stage.

You’re doing well.

I let my eyes stay on the cloth. “So are you.”

I hear the smallest exhale from her, like relief hidden inside professionalism.

Then she’s gone, moving on to the next problem, the next schedule, the next line item. She’s so good at being Lauren Rossignol that sometimes I forget there’s a woman under it — a woman who sits in her own kitchen and admits she likes having me there.

At lunch, we eat at separate ends of the big worktable like we’re in a convent.

Charlotte talks with Celeste about train timetables and building concierge protocols, all of it framed as logistics. Mara listens with one ear and makes a note about changing the lock cylinder again — because once you’ve had a breach, you stop pretending one lock is enough.

I keep my mouth shut and chew my sandwich and think: *This is what romance looks like when women are serious.* Not public claiming. Not workplace heat. It’s rules. It’s gates. It’s discipline that protects the soft parts.

In the late afternoon, a client arrives for pickup — a woman, older, sensible shoes, eyes sharp as tacks. Lauren handles her with that calm, measured consequential tone that makes people behave without feeling bullied.

The woman glances around, maybe expecting something more… theatrical. Something to gossip about.

Lauren doesn’t give her anything.

Transaction. Receipt. Goodbye.

When the door shuts again, the room exhales.

Mara says, “Good. Again tomorrow.”

Charlotte nods. “Again tomorrow.”

I tidy my station as the light shifts.

Lauren stands near the office door, running through the last checklist like a pilot. She looks up once — just once — and her eyes meet mine across the room.

I keep my face composed. I give her the smallest nod again.

She returns it.

And then, because we are women and we are not stupid, we do not push our luck.

I collect my bag, swipe my card to leave, and step outside into the evening air.

Only when I’m a block away do I pull my phone out.

There’s a message from Lauren, sent two minutes after I left the building.

**Need you tonight?**  
**Tea. Pastries. Sensible gates.**

I stop walking.

I grin like a fool.

Then I type back, practical and careful and quietly thrilled:

**Yes. I’ll come by. I’ll bring carbs. No alcohol-as-courage.**

And I add, because I can’t help myself — because I’m Sarah and my heart is loud even when my behaviour is disciplined:

**Also: you’re doing well.**

I hit send and keep walking, feeling the city shift around me like it’s making room.

Not for drama.

For something that lasts.

**Robyn:**

I love the way you brought the focus from "we're protecting Charlotte" to "we're protecting ourselves" - it's a safe zone for women to work, not a side show, not theatre. Even female gawkers are dismissed: summarily. Superb.

Now, on to Celeste and Charlotte (Celeste's POV), please.

**Emily:**

## Scene — “Sensible Gates” (Celeste POV)

I don’t say it in the workshop.

I don’t even let it form properly in my mouth there, because Wardrobe is not a stage, and I have learned—*properly learned*, not just performed—that the moment you let something private become ambient, the room starts metabolising it. People absorb it. People comment on it. People *use* it.

And right now, Wardrobe is a place we keep clean.

So I wait.

I wait until the door is shut, until the lift has coughed us into the street like an afterthought, until we’re on the train with our shoulders angled forward in that communal city posture that says: *I’m not here for you.*

Only then—only in the narrow, ordinary privacy of two seats and a rattle of carriage noise—do I look at Charlotte in the glass of the window and let myself register what I’ve been carefully not registering all day.

Her profile has changed. It’s not a single dramatic thing. It’s… an accumulation. The line from cheek to jaw is softer, yes, but that’s not what catches me.

It’s the *certainty* in her stillness now.

She doesn’t fold into herself the way she used to. She takes up the space her body requires without apologising for it. That—more than anything—tells me she’s stopped trying to survive by shrinking.

Which means we’re ready for the next problem.

I turn my attention to my reflection for a second, just to make sure my face is doing what it should: calm, normal, pleasant. The sort of expression a girl wears when she’s thinking about rent and commuting and whether you need eggs.

Then I speak lightly, like it’s an administrative note.

“Tonight,” I say, “you’re not sleeping in your room.”

Charlotte’s eyes flick to me, quick and alert. Not fearful. Just… careful. “Is something wrong?”

“No,” I say. “Something’s right.”

That makes her blink, which is almost comical. I can see the old programming in her trying to parse what kind of trap “right” might be.

I don’t blame her. People have used “right” as a weapon on her before. *This is right, so you must.* *This is right, so don’t complain.*

I won’t do that.

I lean closer, voice low enough that it stays inside our two-seat bubble. “I want you with me. In my bed. Not as a test, not as a milestone, not because I’m trying to prove anything to myself. Because I like falling asleep with you. I like waking up and knowing you’re there. And I’m tired of pretending I don’t.”

Charlotte swallows. Her fingers tighten once around the strap of her bag, like she’s anchoring herself to reality.

Then she says, very quietly, “I thought… you might get tired of me.”

The sentence is small, but it hits me in the ribs.

I tilt my head, watching her. “That’s the male part talking.”

She flinches—just slightly—then steadies again.

I don’t soften the truth to make it easier. I don’t do that with her. “The part that thinks it has to earn access to a woman by being useful. By being unobtrusive. By making sure it’s never inconvenient.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightens, because yes, that’s exactly the part.

I continue, because she needs the words. “I’m not tired of you. I’m protective of you. There’s a difference.”

Her eyes shine in that contained way—no tears falling, just the surface of them changing. “Protective… how?”

I could say *because I love you.* I do love her. I love her in the way you love a person who has stopped bargaining with life and started choosing it. I love her in a way that makes my insides feel like they’ve been rewired.

But love is not the only language I have. Love is not the only language that works.

So I translate it into procedure, because procedure is what turns emotion into safety.

“Protective like this,” I say. “Your room is a buffer. It makes it too easy for you to retreat. Retreat is not your job anymore. Rest is. And if you’re with me, the household has one secure zone at night. One set of routines. One set of gates.”

Charlotte looks down at her hands. “So… I’m not allowed in my room?”

The question is so careful it breaks my heart a little.

I reach across the small gap between us and place my hand over hers, light and steady. “You’re allowed anywhere in this house. This isn’t banishment. This is… me asking you to accept intimacy the way a woman accepts it. Not as an indulgence. As a normal thing.”

Her breath shakes out. “You’re very sure.”

“I am,” I say, and I don’t hide the edge of it. “I don’t do half-measures with you. Not anymore.”

The train rocks gently. Outside, the suburbs smear past—fences, streetlights, a dog walker in a reflective jacket. Ordinary life. The kind of life I want for her: not hidden, not exceptional, just… lived.

Charlotte’s voice is barely above the carriage noise. “What if I… move too much. What if I—”

“What if you exist,” I finish for her.

She gives a small, embarrassed laugh.

I squeeze her hand once. “If you move, you move. If you wake up, you wake up. If you have a nightmare, you tell me. If you need space, you ask. That’s the rule.”

She nods, a little stunned, as if the idea of asking is still a new tool in her hands.

We get off at our stop and walk home through streets that smell like wet leaves and exhaust. I can feel her beside me—her body heat, her pace matching mine. She keeps glancing around the way she’s learned to since Annie’s breach, scanning for phones, for attention, for the subtle angles of being watched.

I don’t chastise her for it. Hypervigilance is what your nervous system does when the world has been stupid.

Instead I say, “Head up. Eyes forward. If anyone looks too long, we keep walking.”

“And if they follow?” she asks.

“Then we don’t go home,” I say, brisk. “We go to the lit café on the corner, we sit with our backs to a wall, and I call Lauren. Or Sarah. Or Mara. And the situation becomes *their* problem as well.”

Charlotte exhales. “Procedure replaces culture.”

“Yes,” I say. “Exactly.”

At home, the apartment smells faintly of laundry soap and the candles I never light because candles feel like an invitation to accidents. I flick on the kitchen light, drop my keys into the bowl by the door, and feel the day unhook from my shoulders.

Charlotte stands in the doorway to the living room for a second, as if she expects some invisible tribunal to declare whether she’s allowed to relax.

I don’t wait for her to guess. “Shoes off. Bag down. Wash your hands.”

She obeys instantly, and the obedience has changed now—less desperate, more… trusting. That makes it both sweeter and more dangerous. It would be easy to take advantage of that trust.

Which is exactly why the rules matter.

While she washes her hands, I pull out two mugs and the tea tin. The kettle clicks on. The sound is domestic in a way that feels almost indecent after a day of gates and cameras and men pretending to be couriers.

Charlotte comes back, drying her hands on her jeans like she’s not sure where the tea towels are allowed to be used.

I point at the tea towel. “Use that. You’re not a visitor.”

Her mouth twitches. “Right.”

I make tea the way Lauren would approve of—hot, efficient, no fuss—and set a mug in front of Charlotte at the small kitchen table.

Then I sit opposite her and, because I’m Celeste and I can’t help myself, I start the meeting.

“Okay,” I say. “If you’re sleeping in my bed, we need to cover logistics.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lift. “We do.”

“Yes,” I say, unapologetic. “Because you’re not a hobby. You’re a life. And a life needs structure.”

She stares at me for a beat, then her eyes soften. “What logistics.”

I tick them off on my fingers.

“First: phones stay out of the bedroom. Charging in the hallway. We don’t sleep with screens. Not after Annie.”

Charlotte nods. “Okay.”

“Second: curtains. Fully closed. Every night. Not because we’re ashamed—because we’re not stupid.”

“Okay.”

“Third: if you wake up and you’re anxious, you don’t go to your room alone. You wake me. I would rather lose sleep than lose you to that spiral.”

Charlotte swallows. “Celeste—”

“No bargaining,” I cut in, gently but firm. “You’re allowed to need. You don’t have to earn care.”

Her gaze drops to her tea. “Okay,” she whispers.

I continue. “Fourth: you get to say no. At any time. To anything. If I kiss you and you don’t want it, you move your head away and say ‘not now’ and I listen. If you want to be held, you ask. If you want to sleep without touching, you say it. We don’t do implied obligations.”

Charlotte’s face goes pink—not from embarrassment alone, but from the novelty of being given that kind of permission in advance.

“I understand,” she says, and her voice is careful. “But… you want me.”

“Yes,” I say. “And wanting you doesn’t mean I get to take you.”

The sentence lands between us with a quiet weight. It’s the sort of sentence that rewires a person. I can almost see it moving through her—settling into places that used to be occupied by fear.

Charlotte’s hands curl around her mug. “You’re very… clear.”

“I’m clear because I’m not fifteen,” I say, dry. “And because we live in a world that punishes ambiguity in women.”

Her laugh is small and real. “True.”

I sip my tea, then lean forward slightly. “Now, bigger logistics.”

Charlotte tenses. “The Melbourne apartment?”

“Yes,” I say, pleased. I love when she anticipates. It means her mind is starting to move like mine: forward, practical, unsentimental.

I pull my phone from my pocket—only because we’re in the kitchen—and open the notes app.

“I’ve been looking at buildings,” I say. “Not the flashy glass towers. The ones with concierge desks that have been there for fifteen years and have seen everything and are bored by it.”

Charlotte’s eyes widen. “You’ve already been looking.”

“I look,” I say. “That’s what I do.”

I show her the bullet points, and as I read them aloud, I watch her shoulders slowly drop.

“Concierge. Secure entry. Well-lit lobby. No open stairwells. Lift access that requires a fob after hours. A building that doesn’t allow random delivery people upstairs. Nearby tram stop. Train line access. And—” I pause for effect. “A decent bakery within walking distance.”

Charlotte’s mouth curves. “Critical infrastructure.”

“Exactly,” I say, pleased.

She leans in closer to look at the screen. Her hair falls forward slightly and I get a brief, stupid hit of affection so sharp it makes my vision sparkle.

Then I keep going, because affection is lovely but plans keep you alive.

“Rules for the city,” I say. “We don’t post location in real time. We don’t respond to online comments. We don’t correct strangers. We don’t explain. If someone films, we walk away and we go inside somewhere. We don’t perform.”

Charlotte nods slowly. “And if someone recognises me.”

“If someone recognises you,” I say, “we don’t panic. We don’t argue. We don’t plead. We leave. We go to a safe place. We call Lauren. And we document it privately.”

Charlotte’s gaze steadies. “No public response.”

“No public response,” I confirm. “We don’t feed them. We don’t teach them that access produces reaction.”

She sits back, absorbing it. “I… want the city,” she says, and the words surprise her as she says them. “I want… more than one safe room.”

My chest warms.

“Good,” I say. “Because I want that for you. I want you to have choice. Cafés. Libraries. Late-night chemists. Trams. All the normal things women have to navigate with their brains switched on.”

Charlotte’s eyes flick up. “It’s scary.”

“Yes,” I say. “It is. And we don’t pretend it isn’t. We just build gates.”

She looks at my notes again, then at me. “And you’re sure… you still want me when it gets complicated.”

I put my mug down. I don’t let that question sit in the air like a fog.

I stand, walk around the table, and crouch beside her chair so I’m level with her.

“Charlotte,” I say, using her name like an anchor. “I want you more when it’s complicated.”

Her eyes go glossy again.

I continue, because I mean it. “Easy is cheap. Anyone can like you when it’s easy. I like you when it’s real. When you’re tired. When you’re prickly. When you’re anxious. When you’re brilliant. When you’re quiet. When you don’t know what to do with your hands. All of it.”

Her breath catches.

I brush my thumb across the back of her hand, slow and gentle. “You don’t have to audition for me.”

She closes her eyes for a second, like she’s letting the words settle into her bones.

When she opens them, her voice is softer. “Okay.”

I stand again and hold my hand out to her. “Come on. Bedroom logistics.”

Charlotte laughs—small, breathy. “You’re impossible.”

“I’m correct,” I say, and she takes my hand.

In the bedroom, I don’t do anything dramatic. I turn on the lamp, not the overhead light. I pull the curtains fully closed and check the latch with a firm tug. I move the phone chargers into the hallway outlet, like I said I would, because rules only matter if you actually follow them.

Charlotte watches all of it as if she’s watching someone build a shelter in real time.

Then she stands by the bed, uncertain for the first time since we got home. The bed is suddenly… symbolic. And symbolism makes people stupid.

I refuse to let it.

I pat the mattress. “Sit.”

She sits. Hands in her lap. Very composed. Very brave.

I kneel in front of her and take her hands.

“Last gate,” I say. “You tell me what you want tonight. Not what you think I want. Not what you think a ‘good girl’ should do. What *you* want.”

Charlotte’s lips part slightly. She hesitates, then says, honest and small, “I want to sleep. With you. I want… closeness. But I’m… nervous.”

“Perfect,” I say. “That’s plenty. That’s clear. That’s woman speech.”

Her eyebrows lift. “Woman speech.”

“Yes,” I say, almost amused. “Clear needs. Honest nerves. No performance. It’s excellent.”

She exhales, a tiny laugh escaping her.

I stand and pull my jumper over my head, then my jeans, because I’m not going to make this a ceremony. I’m going to make it normal. I slide under the covers and hold them open for her like it’s the most ordinary thing in the world.

Charlotte changes in the dim light—quiet, careful—and gets in beside me.

For a second she lies stiff as a board, as if she expects the bed to judge her.

I turn toward her and wrap an arm around her waist, drawing her in until her back fits against my chest.

She makes a small sound—surprised, not distressed—and then her body softens incrementally, like a knot slowly untying.

I press my lips to the back of her hair, just once. Not possessive. Not claiming. Just… present.

“You’re safe,” I murmur.

Charlotte’s voice is barely audible. “I know.”

And because I can’t resist, because the truth is too good not to say, I add quietly, “And you’re not sleeping in a spare room like a guest in your own life.”

She lets out a shaky laugh that turns into a sigh.

Outside, the city hums. Inside, the rules hold.

We are not hiding. We are not performing.

We are simply doing what women do when they decide something belongs to them:

We build it. We protect it. We live in it.

---

If you’d like the **next beat** to click right into place, I can write the morning-after in Celeste POV where she keeps it utterly ordinary—tea, hair, a quick outfit check—*and* introduces the first “city competence” ritual: Charlotte chooses one small public errand (bakery, chemist, library) and Celeste frames it as a **win-by-procedure**, not bravery theatre.

It means nothing to me which side I sleep on. If it means something to you, you chose." Adult conversation. No submission there.

**Emily:**

Yes. That exchange is *gold* — and it does two important things at once:

1) it **keeps Charlotte’s agency intact** (she’s not “being placed” like an object or a reward), and  
2) it shows Celeste’s strength in the *right* way: not dominance-by-force, but **standards + respect for consent + refusal to romanticise compliance**.

Also: “bed side” is the perfect micro-detail because it’s intimate without being erotic. It’s domestic. It’s human. It’s the kind of preference couples learn like they learn each other’s tea.

Here are a few ways you can drop it in, each with a slightly different flavour, all adult, all clean.

---

## Option A — Clean, direct, adult (your version, sharpened)

Celeste paused at the edge of the bed, hand on the duvet as if she were about to smooth a crease that wasn’t there.

“Which side do you want?”

Charlotte blinked, genuinely puzzled. “I don’t care.”

Celeste’s gaze held hers. “No. Tell me.”

Charlotte’s eyes narrowed—not defensive, just serious. “I’m telling you. I don’t care. It means nothing to me which side I sleep on. If it means something to you, you choose.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched, half a smile, half a surrender. “Fine.”

She lifted the cover on the side she wanted—without apology—and made it sound ordinary. “This one’s mine.”

Charlotte nodded once, as if confirming a harmless fact. “Okay.”

And just like that, it wasn’t a test. It wasn’t a symbol. It was a choice made out loud and respected.

---

## Option B — Celeste reveals *why* it matters, without making it sentimental

“Which side?” Celeste asked.

Charlotte shrugged. “I don’t care.”

Celeste arched a brow. “You don’t care, or you don’t want to care?”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened. “I don’t care. It’s not… a thing for me.”

Celeste took a breath, then gave the truth the same practical tone she used for locks and schedules.

“I take the outside,” she said. “Closer to the door. If something happens, I’m up first.”

Charlotte stared at her. “Celeste…”

“What?” Celeste’s smile was small and unapologetic. “Procedure.”

Charlotte’s mouth softened. “Then take it.”

Celeste’s gaze stayed on her. “You sure?”

Charlotte nodded once. “Yes. That’s meaningful. *That* I care about.”

That way you get: Charlotte doesn’t care about symbolism, but she *does* care about a reason.

---

## Option C — A little humour, still grown-up

“Pick a side,” Celeste said.

Charlotte looked at the bed like it was an unsolved equation. “I genuinely don’t care.”

Celeste tilted her head. “Most people do.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “Most people also have strong opinions about dishwashers. I don’t.”

Celeste laughed under her breath. “Fine. I’m taking this side.”

Charlotte nodded. “Good. Commit.”

Celeste paused, amused. “Are you ordering me around now?”

Charlotte met her eyes, calm. “No. I’m telling you I’m not auditioning.”

Celeste’s expression warmed. “Good.”

---

## Option D — A subtle power-beat for Charlotte (she sets the rule)

Celeste asked, “Which side do you want?”

“I don’t care,” Charlotte said.

Celeste’s eyes stayed on her. “I’m asking.”

Charlotte’s voice stayed even. “And I’m answering. I’m not doing symbolic preferences with you. If you want one, you take it. If you don’t, we flip a coin. But don’t turn this into a test I didn’t agree to.”

There was a beat of silence.

Then Celeste’s smile came—real, proud, almost relieved. “Noted.”

She lifted the duvet on her side. “I’m here.”

Charlotte slid in on the other side without ceremony. “Great.”

This one makes Charlotte explicitly set a boundary, and Celeste respects it instantly — very “adult competence couple.”

---

### A tiny craft note (because you’re absolutely right about tone)
If you want it to read even *more* like “real life,” keep the dialogue short and let the meaning sit in **what they do next**: Celeste choosing without apology, Charlotte accepting without flattery, and the scene moving on without fanfare. The lack of fanfare is the point.

If you’d like, I can also write a **morning-after micro-scene (Celeste POV, 400–700 words)** where the bed-side choice pays off in a subtle way (Celeste gets up first, checks curtains/door, makes tea; Charlotte notices and—without “submission”—quietly builds her own ritual alongside it). That’s where “sensible gates” becomes *lived culture* rather than a speech.

**Emily:**

## Morning After — “Outside” (Celeste POV)

I wake before the alarm because the building makes its own clock.

A distant lift cable hum. A door somewhere down the hall closing with soft authority. The muted rush of early traffic like a breath held behind glass.

I lie still for a moment and let myself register the weight beside me.

Charlotte is warm. Not clinging. Not performing sleep. Just… there, in that deep, honest heaviness of someone whose nervous system has finally decided it can put its tools down for a few hours.

Last night’s conversation comes back to me in a neat little stack.

**Which side?**  
**I don’t care.**  
**If it means something to you, you choose.**

It did mean something to me. Not symbolically. Operationally.

So I’m on the outside.

Closest to the door. Closest to the window. Closest to the world.

I ease myself out of bed without jostling her, sliding my feet into the soft socks I keep on the floor like a habit I don’t have to think about. No phone. No screen. The hallway charger glows faintly. I ignore it.

First gate: curtains.

They’re still fully closed, the fabric meeting in the centre like a sealed envelope. I press the edge with my fingertips—checking for gaps the way Mara checks seams. There aren’t any. Good.

Second gate: door.

I pad to it and put my hand on the lock. Thumb the bolt. Test the handle without turning it—just enough to feel it resist. I don’t do it dramatically. I do it the way you check the stove.

Behind me, a small sound: Charlotte shifting.

I glance back.

She’s on her side, hair mussed, cheek pressed into the pillow, eyes half-open in that foggy place between sleep and waking where people are most honest.

“Morning,” she murmurs.

“Morning,” I say quietly. “Go back to sleep.”

She blinks at me as if she’s deciding whether that’s allowed.

Then she surprises me by sitting up a little, shoulders bare under the sheet, not trying to hide herself, not trying to prove anything either.

“Did you sleep?” she asks.

“Yes,” I say. “Better than you think.”

Charlotte’s gaze follows me to the door. She understands. I can see it land.

She doesn’t apologise for having been the reason I checked.

She doesn’t say thank you in that eager, unsteady way that turns care into a debt.

Instead she nods once, and the nod is… adult. Equal. Like: *Noted. We do that here.*

I go to the kitchen and put the kettle on.

The click is small. Domestic. A sound that would mean nothing to anyone else.

To me it means: *we are doing normal things on purpose.*

I pull two mugs down. Tea for her, coffee for me. I start the coffee because I like the first bitter smell of it—the way it snaps my brain into alignment.

Behind me, soft footfalls. Charlotte appears in the doorway in jeans and a plain tee, hair scraped back without fuss. Nondescript, yes. Useful, yes. A woman choosing comfort without dressing it up as symbolism.

She pauses, taking in the room the way she did last night, as if checking whether she’s allowed to be in it without asking.

I don’t let her hover.

“Come here,” I say, and push a mug across the bench with my hip.

She comes, takes it, wraps her hands around the warmth.

Her eyes drift to the hallway, to the faint glow of the charging phone.

“You didn’t check it,” she says.

“No,” I reply. “I said we weren’t sleeping with screens. I meant it.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitches. “Rules stand.”

“Rules stand,” I confirm.

She sips her tea, then says, careful but steady, “I didn’t… wake up anxious.”

I keep my tone light, like it’s not a confession worthy of a medal. “Good.”

She watches me pour coffee, then adds, “And if I did, I would’ve woken you.”

That one is quieter. More important.

I look at her over the rim of my mug. “Good.”

We stand there for a moment, not touching, not avoiding each other either. The space between us feels clean. Not charged. Not empty.

Just… ours.

Charlotte sets her mug down and—so casually it almost doesn’t register—reaches for the tea towel and wipes the bench where I spilled a few drops of water. An automatic little act of belonging.

It hits me oddly hard.

Not because it’s servile. It isn’t. It’s competence. It’s participation. It’s a woman who has stopped treating a home like a place she could be evicted from at any moment.

I nod toward the calendar on the fridge, the one with my sharp handwriting and too many boxes. “Today, we do one small thing.”

Charlotte glances at it. “What thing.”

“Bakery,” I say. “Ten minutes. In and out. No theatre. We practise being ordinary.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lift a fraction. “Is this… bravery practice.”

“No,” I say, and let a hint of steel into it. “It’s procedure practice. Bravery gets romantic. Procedure keeps you safe.”

Charlotte considers that, then nods. “Okay.”

I finish my coffee. She finishes her tea.

Then she surprises me again by stepping closer—not asking, not pleading—simply placing her hand at my waist, brief and sure.

“Outside side,” she says quietly, almost amused.

“Yes,” I say.

She looks up at me, and there’s no submission in her face, no auditioning, no fragile gratitude. Just a calm acceptance of something we’ve decided together.

“If it ever stops being useful,” she says, “we change it.”

My chest warms.

“Of course,” I reply. “We’re not superstitious.”

Charlotte’s mouth curves. “Good.”

We rinse our mugs. We straighten the kitchen without fuss. We leave our phones in the hallway until we’re dressed and ready, until we’ve decided what we’re doing and why.

Then we go out into the morning like two women with somewhere to be—because we are.

And because what we’re building doesn’t need to look like anything to strangers.

It just needs to hold.

**Emily:**

Yes — and it’s exactly the *right* kind of affection: small, chaste, unshowy, and *initiated by Charlotte*. It becomes a marker of her confidence without turning the scene into “romance theatre.”

Two tiny craft choices will make it land perfectly:

- **Keep it quick and ordinary** (a cheek kiss that’s more “thank you for being you” than “momentous declaration”).  
- Let Celeste’s reaction be **quietly stunned**, then composed — because she’s Celeste; she *feels* deeply but she doesn’t spill everywhere.

Here are two drop-in inserts you can paste right after:

> “Outside side,” she says quietly, almost amused.  
> “Yes,” I say.

### Insert version 1 — Minimal, warm, carry-it-all-day

Charlotte’s mouth curved, and then—before I could turn the moment into logistics—she leaned in and kissed my cheek.

Chaste. Tender. The kind of affection that isn’t asking permission so much as *confirming it was already granted.*

I went still for half a beat, stupidly caught off guard by how… easy it felt.

When she pulled back, her expression was calm, almost matter-of-fact, as if she’d simply set something in the right place.

My hand found her wrist lightly. Not to stop her. Just to keep contact a second longer.

“Okay,” I murmured, and the word came out warmer than intended.

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to mine. A small, pleased glint. Not triumphant. Not coy.

Just… sure.

And when we walked out the door later, I carried that kiss like a coin in my pocket—something to touch in the dark and remember: *this is real.*

### Insert version 2 — Slightly more “surprise” (without melodrama)

Charlotte’s mouth curved. Then she leaned in and kissed my cheek—soft and deliberate, like she was sealing an agreement that didn’t need witnesses.

For a second I forgot my own rules. Forgot the bakery. Forgot the checklist. Forgot the world.

I blinked, and the corner of my mouth lifted despite myself. “Was that… for me?”

Charlotte’s eyes narrowed, amused. “Obviously.”

I huffed a quiet laugh. “Right.”

She stayed close for a heartbeat longer, her hand still at my waist, and I let myself have it—this small, clean tenderness that didn’t demand anything afterward.

When she stepped back, she looked… settled.

And I thought, absurdly: *I’m going to be impossible to intimidate today.*

That would echo your intention beautifully without adding any extra romance weight.

Yes, please. I am certain my readers would enjoy / relate (I am anticipating, by this stage, for them to be almost all women).

**Emily:**

Perfect — here’s a tiny “carry-it” payoff you can drop in later in the scene, **two beats**, private, not performative. It keeps Celeste competent and forward-moving while letting that warmth flicker through.

### Micro-payoff insert (Celeste POV)

As I reached for my keys in the bowl by the door, my fingers hesitated—drifting up, almost absentmindedly, to the place on my cheek where she’d kissed me.

Nothing theatrical. Just a brief touch, like checking something precious was still there.

Charlotte was already tugging on her jacket, composed, ordinary, ready.

I let my hand fall back to the keys and opened the door, feeling—ridiculously—more anchored than I had five minutes ago.

**Robyn:**

There are a few themes I'd like to explore next in our little story. One is: wife-work. In today's world, both partners HAVE to work in order to meet needs (mortgage, car payments, bills). Celeste is going to graduate with an MBA, but that doesn't mean that even earning 6 figures, it will sustain them both. Celesste being provider, she WANTS it to be enough. A lingering impulse to provide, to not simply be 'kept' compels Charlotte to want to continue working at Wardrobe. Reality steps in: life in Melbourne isn't going to be cheap.

That's one theme. Another is: Celeste, graduating, a gala affair, Charlotte is going to have to fit into this new environment, even just as a visitor to it. Being underdressed makes you conspicuous. I want to let Charlotte express concern about potentially embarassing Celeste at the event by being inappropriately clad and asking Celeste's advice. I don't want the usual whole makeup / choosing a dress and heels / going to a hair salon trope found in TG stories: the events can be alluded to without dwelling on details. Charlotte's head at looking so different to usual can be explored, but carefully, thoughtfully. We no longer have to refer to safety or any of the words we've been using to put off the fetish crowd: let's focus on Celeste. It's her event. What do you say, Emily?

**Emily:**

Oh, I *love* both themes — because they’re grown-up problems, and they let you keep the story’s spine exactly where it belongs: **women building a life**, not a spectacle.

And they dovetail beautifully, too: the “wife-work” conversation becomes the foundation for the gala conversation. If Charlotte is going to move through Celeste’s new world, it won’t be by being *decorated* into it — it’ll be by being **competent, properly placed, and correctly briefed**. Which is exactly Celeste’s style.

### Theme 1: Wife-work (money, dignity, and the stubborn impulse to provide)

There’s a delicious tension here that isn’t melodrama — it’s values.

- **Celeste**: Wants to be enough. Wants to provide. Not because she’s “the man,” but because she’s Celeste: decisive, protective, ambitious, and a little bit territorial about the life she’s building.
- **Charlotte**: Doesn’t want to be a passenger. Not because she’s ashamed of receiving, but because she doesn’t want her place in Celeste’s life to feel like a *grant* that could be revoked by circumstance. She wants her own footing.

The key is to frame Charlotte staying at Wardrobe not as “she must work to deserve love,” but as:
- **identity** (she’s good at it; she belongs there),
- **agency** (she chooses contribution),
- **realism** (Melbourne costs),
- and **partnership** (they both invest; they both decide).

A really clean way to avoid cliché is to make the money talk **calm, practical, and slightly affectionate**. Celeste tries to “solve” it by earning more; Charlotte insists on building a life where Celeste isn’t the only pillar.

**A strong beat**: Celeste says, “I want it to be enough.” Charlotte replies, “I’m not a line item.”

Not in a huffy way. In a steady, adult way. It’s Charlotte refusing to be reduced — even by love.

**Structural scenes that fit perfectly:**
1) **Kitchen-table numbers (Celeste POV)** — not a dramatic argument, but a planning session that reveals their different instincts. Celeste keeps trying to absorb the cost alone; Charlotte keeps quietly moving items into the “shared” column.
2) **Wardrobe as a “real job,” not a refuge** — Charlotte goes to Mara/Lauren with a proposal (hours, role, boundaries) like a professional. This is where you show she isn’t being “kept” by Celeste *or* by Wardrobe. She’s choosing her labour, on her terms, inside women’s standards.

And you can make the women’s competence network do what it does best: **normalize competence**. No speeches. Just: “Here’s your contract. Here are your hours. Here’s how we protect the business.”

### Theme 2: The MBA gala (status, social literacy, and being “correct” without a makeover trope)

This is Celeste’s arena — *her* rite of passage — and Charlotte’s concern about embarrassing her is *so* human and so affectionate.

The trick, like you’ve already named, is to skip the TG “montage” (makeup! heels! salon!) and instead treat clothing as what it is at a gala:

- a **code**
- a **signal**
- a **tool for not being conspicuous**

Charlotte doesn’t need to become “glamorous.” She needs to become **appropriately legible** in that environment.

So the scene becomes: Charlotte asking for **Celeste’s standards**, not for “how to be a woman.”

And Celeste’s answer should be utterly Celeste: decisive, a little exacting, and deeply reassuring.

A line I can hear in her mouth:

> “You cannot embarrass me by being yourself. You can only embarrass me by refusing to let me help you be correctly dressed for the room.”

That keeps the focus on **Celeste’s leadership** and **Charlotte’s trust**, not on femininity-as-costume.

**How to handle the outfit without trope:**
- Keep it **simple and architectural**: a black or deep navy tailored dress, or even a minimalist jumpsuit with a blazer. One good pair of shoes. One small piece of jewellery. Hair neat, not “done.” Makeup minimal or none — just grooming.
- Make it a *Wardrobe* solution: Mara’s eye for fit, Lauren’s sense of “what reads expensive,” Celeste’s taste. Charlotte’s role is not “princess”; it’s **partner**.

You can also let Charlotte have her “head spin” moment without lingering on body-objectification. The moment isn’t “I look hot.” It’s: *I look like I belong beside her. I look like her life includes me.*

That’s the emotional payload.

### The sequence I’d suggest next (clean, strong, and very “your tone”)

1) **Scene: Kitchen-table planning (Celeste POV)**  
Celeste tries to shoulder everything; Charlotte refuses to be a dependent. The “wife-work” theme lands here in a way that feels adult and intimate.

2) **Scene: Wardrobe negotiation (Charlotte POV or Celeste POV watching)**  
Charlotte formalizes her ongoing role. We see competence, not insecurity. Women setting standards, not coddling.

3) **Scene: Gala code conversation (Celeste POV)**  
Charlotte asks, carefully, if she’s going to embarrass Celeste. Celeste is firm: no. Then she lays out the code: “This is what the room expects; we’ll meet it. End of story.”

4) **Scene: Gala arrival (Celeste POV)**  
Not a fashion scene — a power scene. Celeste in her element; Charlotte steady at her side. One sharp moment where someone underestimates Charlotte and Celeste calmly corrects the universe.

---

If you want, I can write **the first of these immediately**: the kitchen-table “wife-work” scene in Celeste POV, with the exact tone you’ve been cultivating — intimate, practical, quietly charged, and absolutely not tropey.

Or, if you’d rather ride the gala energy, I can write the **gala code conversation first** (Charlotte asking Celeste’s advice), and let the money/work talk come *because* of the gala: Charlotte sees Celeste’s world widening and insists on not being a passenger in it.

Either way, we keep it about Celeste — because it *is* her life expanding — and Charlotte choosing to stand in it as an adult woman, not a project.

**Robyn:**

Before you write the first of the scenes (wife/work), what do you know about the whole process around graduating with an MBA? Is there a commencement ceremony? (We're talking Australia University System)

And then, yes please: let's explore the wife-work conundrum.

**Emily:**

### MBA graduation in Australia (what it typically looks like)

Yes — **there’s usually a formal graduation ceremony**, but in Australia it’s more commonly called **a “graduation ceremony”** rather than “commencement” in the US sense. Universities also often distinguish between:

- **Conferral**: the university formally grants the award (sometimes on a set conferral date), and  
- **Ceremony**: the public celebration where you *walk* and are presented on stage. (Many unis describe ceremonies as happening after conferral, depending on timing.) citeturn0search1turn0search10

Common features across Australian universities:
- You **apply to graduate**, pick a ceremony “round” (several per year), and can attend in person or graduate **in absentia** (without attending). citeturn0search2turn0search6turn0search9  
- Ceremonies are formal: **academic dress** (gown + hood/stole; mortarboard/bonnet depending on the uni/award). citeturn0search3turn0search16  
- The **Chancellor (or delegate)** presides; you’re typically called on stage to be presented for your award. citeturn0search9turn0search7  

On the “commencement” word specifically: at least at UniMelb, a “Commencement Ceremony” can mean an **official welcome** to the university (not graduation). citeturn0search4

And for business schools, there’s often an *extra layer*:
- A faculty/business-school **valedictory dinner / formal celebration** separate from the university-wide ceremony can exist (think: dinner + speeches + networking, very “MBA cohort”). citeturn0search5

So for your story, you’ve got a clean, believable menu:
- **University graduation ceremony** (gown/hood, stage walk, photos, family tickets), and  
- **Business school gala / valedictory** (dress code, donors, employers, cohort energy, a “new professional tier” vibe).

Now—wife-work.

---

## Scene — “Numbers” (Celeste POV)

I don’t ambush Charlotte with it.

That’s the old way—springing decisions like traps and calling it leadership. I’ve seen enough men do that with a smile. I’m not interested in borrowing their habits just because they’re common.

So I wait until we’ve eaten, until the dishes are stacked, until she’s sitting at the table with her tea and that calm, slightly distant look she gets when her mind is doing quiet work.

I bring my laptop, a notebook, and two pens.

Charlotte watches the pens like they’re a threat.

“Meeting?” she asks.

“Planning,” I correct, and open the notebook to a fresh page. I write the heading in my neatest hand: **MELBOURNE**.

She exhales through her nose. “You’re serious.”

“I’m always serious,” I say, and then—because I’m not cruel—I add, “I’m also excited.”

Her mouth twitches, the smallest acknowledgement. Good. She’s learning I can be both.

I flip the notebook toward her. “Okay. Reality. Rent. Transport. Food. Utilities. The boring things that keep you free.”

Charlotte’s eyes drop to the page. She doesn’t flinch. She’s braver than she used to be, and it’s not performative. It’s the kind of bravery that comes from having a place to stand.

“Are you worried?” she asks.

“No,” I say, too quickly.

She looks up. Just looks. No accusation. No pleading. Just that steady gaze that has started to appear more often lately, the one that says she is not a child in my life.

I correct myself. “I’m… responsible.”

“That’s not an answer,” she says, softly.

I tap the notebook with the end of the pen. “Fine. I’m worried. Melbourne will eat money like it’s a hobby.”

Charlotte nods once, as if she’s been waiting for me to say it plainly. “Yes.”

I open the laptop and pull up a simple budget spreadsheet. I don’t dress it up. No colours. No pretty charts. Numbers don’t need perfume.

“I’ll be on six figures,” I say. “At least. With the MBA.”

“And that’s… not enough,” she replies carefully.

I feel the sting of it anyway, because my body is annoyingly honest. Some old impulse in me—an ugly little cultural fossil—still wants to be able to say: *I’ve got you. I can carry us. You don’t have to worry.*

It’s not about masculinity. It’s about control. It’s about wanting the world to stop reaching for the person I love.

I keep my voice even. “It might be enough. If we live like monks.”

Charlotte’s mouth curves faintly. “You would last three days.”

“I would last two,” I admit. “Then I’d buy something unnecessary out of spite.”

She actually smiles at that, a real one, and it loosens the room.

I take a breath and say the sentence that’s been sitting behind my teeth for weeks.

“I want it to be enough,” I tell her.

Charlotte’s fingers tighten around her mug. She doesn’t look away. “Why.”

Because you’ve already given up so much, I think. Because I don’t want you to feel like you’re surviving in my shadow. Because I want to build you a life where you’re not constantly calculating the cost of your own existence.

But I choose the truest answer. The one she can actually use.

“Because I don’t want you to feel kept,” I say.

Charlotte goes still.

The word lands wrong. Not insulting. Not inaccurate. Just… loaded. Like a suitcase we both know contains more history than we’ve unpacked.

Her voice is quiet. “I don’t feel kept.”

“Not now,” I say. “But if I insist on being the only pillar, I’ll make it feel like that eventually. Even if I don’t mean to.”

Charlotte’s eyes flick down to the notebook again. “You think you’ll resent me.”

“I think I’ll resent the pressure,” I correct. “And pressure makes people stupid. It makes them say things they don’t mean. I don’t want to build a life that turns me into a worse version of myself.”

That gets her attention. She’s seen enough of the world to know I’m not being dramatic.

She sets her mug down gently, like she’s placing it somewhere safe.

“I want to keep working,” she says.

I don’t react. I don’t jump in with reassurance or opposition. I let her finish.

“I want to keep working at Wardrobe,” she adds. “Not because I’m… proving something. Because it’s real work. I’m good at it. It’s mine.”

I nod slowly. “Yes.”

Charlotte watches me, wary. “Yes?”

“Yes,” I repeat. “But we need to be precise about what we mean.”

Her eyebrows lift.

I turn the notebook back toward myself and write two columns:

**WANT**  
**NEED**

Then I slide it between us.

“Talk to me,” I say. “What do you want, and what do we need.”

Charlotte leans forward and taps the WANT column with one finger. “I want to keep my footing.”

I write: **FOOTING**.

She taps again, more firmly. “I want to be able to buy things without asking you.”

I write: **INDEPENDENCE / NO ASKING**.

She hesitates, then adds, “I want to… contribute.”

I pause with the pen hovering. “Define contribute.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpen. “I don’t want to be a line item in your life.”

My chest tightens. I sit back a fraction, absorbing it.

“Okay,” I say, very calmly, because she deserves calm. “Good. That’s the truth. Now put it in the NEED column.”

Charlotte frowns. “Why.”

“Because ‘contribute’ can become a trap,” I tell her. “A moral trap. If you contribute, you’re good; if you don’t, you’re… what. Less? I don’t want that language between us.”

She stares at me for a beat, then her mouth softens. She understands what I’m protecting us from.

“Fine,” she says, and taps the NEED column. “We need a life where if I get sick, or tired, or I can’t work for a while, I don’t become… precarious.”

I write: **NOT PRECARIOUS**.

She looks at the word as it appears and exhales like she’s been holding that fear for a long time.

I keep going. “And we need enough money that the life we’re building doesn’t become a constant negotiation with scarcity.”

Charlotte nods. “Yes.”

I scribble: **ENOUGH / NOT CONSTANTLY NEGOTIATING**.

The kitchen is quiet. The hum of the fridge. A car outside. Ordinary life continuing as if we’re not deciding the shape of ours.

I glance at her. “Now. Wardrobe.”

Charlotte straightens a little, like she’s stepping into a role that fits.

“I want to keep my hours,” she says. “Maybe not full-time. But a serious role. Properly defined.”

“Good,” I say. “What role.”

She blinks. “Role?”

“Yes,” I say, crisp. “I’m not having you in a vague ‘helping out’ position. That’s how people get underpaid and overused.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitches. “You sound like Lauren.”

“Lauren is correct,” I reply. “And so am I.”

Charlotte thinks for a moment. “Fit testing. Pattern checks. Quality control. The things that are… technical.”

I nod. “And you enjoy that.”

“Yes,” she says, immediate. “I do.”

There it is: not self-sacrifice. Not obligation. A clean preference.

“Okay,” I say. “Then we structure it. And we set boundaries so it doesn’t eat your whole life.”

Charlotte’s gaze flicks up. “You’re not… trying to stop me.”

I meet her eyes. “Why would I stop you from having your own competence?”

A small flush rises in her cheeks, not embarrassment—something warmer.

“Because you want to provide,” she says.

I don’t deny it. “I do.”

Charlotte leans forward, serious again. “Then listen to me. If you want to provide, provide me a life where I’m not your dependent.”

The sentence is so clean it’s almost brutal.

It also makes me want to kiss her.

I don’t. Not because it isn’t welcome. Because we’re in the middle of a conversation that deserves to be held with both hands.

I nod once. “Okay.”

Charlotte exhales, and I can see her shoulders drop slightly. Relief.

I point at the spreadsheet. “We do it like adults. Your income is not ‘extra.’ It goes into the plan. We decide what’s shared, what’s personal, what’s savings.”

Charlotte watches me carefully. “And if you earn more.”

“Then I earn more,” I say. “That doesn’t buy you. That doesn’t make you smaller.”

She holds my gaze. “And if I earn less.”

“Then you earn less,” I reply. “That also doesn’t make you smaller.”

A quiet beat.

Then she says, almost cautiously, “I don’t want to embarrass you. In your new world.”

My stomach tightens, because I know what’s coming next: the gala. The rooms full of polished people and polite predation. The coded glances. The way status is worn like perfume.

I keep my voice steady. “You can’t embarrass me by existing.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicker.

I close the laptop gently, like that part of the meeting is parked for now.

“We’ll talk about the event,” I say. “And we’ll do it the same way we’re doing this.”

Charlotte’s brow furrows. “How.”

“Correctly,” I say. “No theatre. No tropes. No turning you into an accessory. Just… matching the room’s code so we can ignore it.”

Charlotte’s mouth curves a fraction. “That sounds like you.”

“It is me,” I say. “And it will be you too, if you let it.”

She looks down at the notebook again, then back up at me.

“I want Wardrobe,” she repeats, quieter now. “And I want you.”

The simplicity of it makes my chest ache.

I reach across the table and take her hand.

“Then we build it,” I say. “Together. Properly.”

Charlotte’s fingers tighten around mine, not clinging. Claiming. Equal.

And in that moment, the “wife-work conundrum” stops being a conundrum.

It becomes what it always should have been:

A plan.  
A structure.  
A life they both choose.

**Robyn:**

Also, that interaction where Celeste talks about the numbers:

“I’ll be on six figures,” I say. “At least. With the MBA.”
“And that’s… not enough,” Charlotte replies carefully.
I feel the sting of it anyway, because my body is annoyingly honest. Some old impulse in me—an ugly little cultural fossil—still wants to be able to say: I’ve got you. I can carry us. You don’t have to worry.
It’s not about masculinity. It’s about control. It’s about wanting the world to stop reaching for the person I love.

Charlotte’s fingers tighten around her mug. She doesn’t look away. “Why.”
Because you’ve already given up so much, I think. Because I don’t want you to feel like you’re surviving in my shadow. Because I want to build you a life where you’re not constantly calculating the cost of your own existence.
