It keeps the focus on **governance-as-care**, gives each woman a *specific* role in scaling, and lets Mara’s health pressure the system without melodrama. I’ll build in “mini-bumps” that feel real but don’t fracture the culture.

---

## Mini-Arc: Scaling + Mara Health  
*(4 scenes, each with: purpose, friction, key beat, and a carry-forward change)*

### Scene 1 — “Capacity Ceiling”
**POV:** Celeste (or Lauren)  
**Purpose:** Establish that growth is real and they need governance, not heroics.  
**Friction:** Celeste brings numbers; Mara hears “loss of control.” Sarah hates meetings but keeps it human.  
**The beat:** Celeste presents a simple model: orders rising, QC risk rising; proposes a capacity ceiling + selective outsourcing *only if* QC is enforceable.  
**Mini-bump:** Mara snaps: “Outsourcing is where standards die.” Sarah needles: “So don’t outsource standards.”  
**Key line (Celeste):** “We’re not scaling output. We’re scaling *correctness*.”  
**Outcome change:** They agree to a **Capacity Ceiling Policy**: if orders exceed X, they **pause intake** or add **pre-approved overflow** with Wardrobe QC sign-off.

---

### Scene 2 — “Near-Miss”
**POV:** Lauren  
**Purpose:** Show Mara’s health as *small failures of perfection*, and why the system must adjust.  
**Friction:** Mara pushes through symptoms and misses something tiny; Lauren catches it, but it scares her.  
**The beat:** A garment almost ships with a hidden stress flaw. Mara catches it at the last moment (or Lauren does), and Mara is furious—at the garment, at time, at weakness.  
**Mini-bump:** Mara lashes out at “distraction” or “sloppiness,” and Sarah fires back with one line that’s funny but edged.  
**Key line (Lauren):** “This isn’t about blame. It’s about chain of custody.”  
**Outcome change:** Lauren quietly starts **Incident Logging v2** (internal): near misses count as data, not shame.

---

### Scene 3 — “Two Signatures”
**POV:** Lauren or Celeste  
**Purpose:** Turn love into policy; show Mara’s resistance and eventual acceptance through procedure.  
**Friction:** Mara calls it bureaucracy; Celeste frames it as continuity; Sarah keeps it from going cold; Lauren doesn’t blink.  
**The beat:** They introduce the **Two-Signature Rule** for shipments and critical QC, plus a defined exception process.  
**Mini-bump:** Mara demands to be the sole authority; Celeste refuses the bottleneck: “Single points of failure are not romantic.”  
**Key line (Celeste):** “A standard that depends on one person isn’t a standard. It’s a risk.”  
**Outcome change:** A new governance artifact exists: **SHIP ONLY WITH TWO SIGN-OFFS** in the ledger + a **QC checklist** attached to every order batch.

---

### Scene 4 — “The Slip”
**POV:** Celeste (or Charlotte, if you want it to land quietly)  
**Purpose:** Make Mara’s health undeniable; show the team rallying without turning it into pity or a lecture.  
**Friction:** Mara tries to hide it. The team refuses the secrecy—not invasively, but firmly.  
**The beat:** Mara has a visible “slip”: dizziness, a hand tremor, a migraine aura—something that makes her stop mid-task. She tries to shrug it off.  
**Mini-bump:** Mara snaps: “I’m fine,” and Sarah’s humour fails on purpose—she goes serious. Lauren steps in with calm authority.  
**Key line (Sarah, quiet):** “We’re not asking. We’re staffing.”  
**Outcome change:** They impose a **Mara Load Rule** (framed as *quality control*, not “care”):  
- Mara no longer does late nights alone.  
- Design sign-off has **time windows**.  
- One day per week is **non-production** for Mara (research / painting / recovery) with zero guilt.

**Closing emotional line (not melodramatic):**  
- Lauren: “You built the ‘us.’ Now you live in it.”  
or  
- Celeste: “Your legacy is a system, Mara. Let it work.”  
or  
- Mara, grudging: “Fine. But don’t get sentimental.”  
- Sarah: “Wouldn’t dream of it.” *(and she means it)*

---

# The mini-bumps (so it feels like a real team)
These are small conflicts that show personality without threatening the culture:

1) **Mara vs. Outsourcing**: Mara treats it as betrayal; Celeste frames it as *controlled overflow with enforceable QC*.  
2) **Sarah vs. Meetings**: Sarah jokes through tension; Lauren snaps once: “Jokes after decisions.” Sarah backs off—she respects command.  
3) **Celeste vs. Control Instinct**: Celeste tries to “fix” too much too fast; Lauren reminds her: “Governance, not domination.”  
4) **Lauren vs. Protective Rage**: Lauren wants to ban everything risky; Charlotte says, calmly: “Living includes risk. We manage it.”

---

# Optional 5th “bridge” scene (if you want Sarah’s role to deepen)
### Scene 5 — “Front Door”
**POV:** Sarah  
**Purpose:** Sarah becomes the culture guard, not just the quip machine.  
**Beat:** Sarah turns away a pushy client / influencer / “curious” person with *terrifying politeness*, protecting Mara’s bandwidth and the team’s focus.  
**Key line (Sarah):** “We’re booked. We’re private. And you’re done.”  
**Outcome:** Sarah is formally given **Front-of-House Authority** (yes, even over Mara in client moments).

---

## How this sets up the next phase
After these scenes, you can cleanly pivot into:
- Celeste stepping into external strategy (partnerships, contracts, supplier audits),
- Lauren becoming operations lead in practice (not just “mum energy”),
- Sarah becoming gatekeeper and morale (with real authority),
- Charlotte moving into QC/wear-testing as a pillar,
- Mara confronting the reality that her standards can survive *without* her hands doing everything.

If you want, I’ll write **Scene 1 (“Capacity Ceiling”)** in full prose next, with the meeting feeling like your world: not corporate fluff—women being precise, a bit sharp, and ultimately aligned.

**Robyn:**

Please write scene 1.

**Emily:**

## Scene 1 — “Capacity Ceiling” (Celeste POV, past tense)

Celeste didn’t call it a meeting.

She called it a table.

That was all Wardrobe ever really did: put the truth on a table and make people look at it until it stopped being scary.

They’d cleared the cutting surface after lunch. Pattern paper rolled up, pins swept into their tin, chalk dust wiped away. The ledger sat at one end like a judge. A laptop sat at the other like an irritant.

Mara stood with her arms folded, already radiating dissatisfaction at the very concept of numbers.

Lauren sat with a notepad and a pen, posture calm, eyes focused, the kind of readiness that made Celeste feel both grateful and faintly challenged.

Sarah leaned against the doorway with a coffee and an expression that suggested she would rather be anywhere else, but would be useful anyway because she was loyal in the only way that mattered: she showed up.

Charlotte hovered near the back of the room, quiet, listening—present enough to count, not so present she felt like she was auditioning.

Celeste set her laptop down and took a breath.

“Okay,” she said. “This won’t take long.”

Sarah immediately lifted her coffee. “Famous last words.”

Celeste didn’t look at her. “If you interrupt, I’ll assign you spreadsheets.”

Sarah made a face like she’d been threatened with violence.

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “She will.”

Mara’s voice was flat. “Proceed.”

Celeste clicked a simple spreadsheet open. No fancy graphs. Just a list. Orders. Dates. Hours. QC time. Returns.

“The US winter orders are not a blip,” Celeste said. “They’ve become a season.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “We’ve always had seasons.”

“Not like this,” Celeste replied. “We’ve doubled the run rate compared to last year, and the lead time has tightened. It’s good. It’s real. But it changes our risk profile.”

Lauren nodded once, already tracking.

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “Risk profile. Listen to you.”

Celeste kept going. “Right now we’re managing it the old way.”

Mara didn’t blink. “The correct way.”

“The heroic way,” Celeste corrected, calmly. “Late nights. Adrenaline. Everyone doing two jobs. It’s not sustainable.”

Mara’s jaw tightened. “Sustainable is a word people use when they want permission to do less.”

Lauren’s pen paused. She didn’t look up yet, but Celeste could feel her attention sharpen.

Celeste didn’t rise to the provocation. She’d learned that with Mara, the fastest route wasn’t winning an argument—it was building an undeniable frame.

“This is not about doing less,” Celeste said. “It’s about controlling standards when volume rises.”

She turned the laptop slightly so they could all see.

“Here,” she said, tapping the screen. “We currently average—” she glanced at her notes, precise, “—X hours per garment line including patterning, cutting, assembly, and finishing. QC adds Y minutes per unit on top.”

Mara made a small sound of irritation. “We don’t measure minutes.”

Lauren looked up. “We do now.”

Sarah’s eyes flicked between them, amused. “Ooh. Mum voice.”

Lauren ignored her. “If we don’t measure time, we don’t know when we’re lying to ourselves.”

Mara’s gaze turned sharp. “We are not a factory.”

Celeste nodded. “Exactly. Which is why we need a ceiling.”

Silence.

The word landed like a challenge. Celeste watched Mara’s face tighten. Watched that familiar resistance rise: *No one tells me what we can’t do.*

Celeste kept her tone steady. “A capacity ceiling isn’t a limitation. It’s a governance tool. It prevents us from taking work we can’t do correctly.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “We can do it.”

Celeste didn’t argue. She offered the consequence.

“Then we do it wrong,” Celeste said. “Or we do it right and burn out. Or we do it right and something breaks—someone breaks—because we’re pretending human bodies are infinite.”

Mara’s jaw flexed.

Sarah, unexpectedly, didn’t crack a joke. She just said, quietly, “She’s not wrong.”

Mara’s eyes cut to her. “You’re on her side.”

Sarah shrugged. “I’m on the side of not collapsing in a heap in October.”

Lauren leaned forward slightly, pen poised. “Define the ceiling.”

Celeste felt relief—a clean click of alignment. This was what she needed: not agreement from Mara first, but structure.

“Two pieces,” Celeste said. “One: we define the maximum weekly output that we can ship without exceptions. Two: we define what happens when demand exceeds it.”

Charlotte spoke from the back, voice soft but clear. “We stop taking orders.”

Mara’s gaze flicked to Charlotte, surprised by her entry into governance.

Celeste nodded at Charlotte. “Yes. Or we triage. Or we extend lead time. But we don’t pretend.”

Mara’s mouth tightened. “Turning away work is stupidity.”

“It’s discipline,” Lauren said. Her voice wasn’t harsh. It was final.

Sarah lifted her coffee again like a toast. “Discipline. Sexy.”

Lauren shot her a look. Sarah subsided, smiling.

Celeste continued. “Here are our options when we hit ceiling:

1) Pause intake for a period.  
2) Raise lead time transparently.  
3) Raise price to slow demand.  
4) Create controlled overflow with pre-approved external support—only if QC remains in-house.”

Mara’s face hardened at the fourth option. “No.”

Celeste held her gaze. “Not outsourcing work. Outsourcing volume. Under our control. Cut-and-sew support, perhaps, but patterns, fit, and final QC stay here.”

Mara’s voice went colder. “That is how standards die.”

Celeste didn’t flinch. “Standards die when they’re implicit. If we codify them, they can survive.”

Mara stared at her as if she’d just committed blasphemy.

Lauren spoke, calm. “Celeste’s right about one thing: if we keep scaling by heroics, we will eventually ship something with our name on it that fails in public.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “It won’t.”

Lauren didn’t blink. “It will, because bodies get tired.”

Sarah nodded slowly, the humour leaving her face for once. “And because people start taking shortcuts without meaning to.”

Charlotte added, quiet, precise. “Fit failures don’t announce themselves until after you’ve worn something for an hour.”

Mara’s mouth tightened. She hated hearing her own truths spoken back to her.

Celeste tapped the ledger with two fingers. “This book is evidence that standards are written down. We already do governance. We just haven’t named it.”

Mara’s gaze flicked to the ledger, then away.

Celeste leaned in slightly. “Mara. I’m not here to take your authority. I’m here to keep your authority from becoming a bottleneck that crushes us.”

Mara’s eyes sharpened. “Authority isn’t meant to be comfortable.”

Celeste let herself smile, a little. “No. But it is meant to be survivable.”

Sarah made a small sound—approval disguised as a cough.

Lauren, efficient, cut through the tension. “We need a number.”

Celeste nodded. She’d come prepared.

She slid a single page onto the table—clean, simple, no fluff.

**CAPACITY CEILING — DRAFT**  
**Weekly output (ship-ready units):** ___  
**QC time per unit:** ___  
**Max concurrent lines:** ___  
**Buffer requirement (for rework):** ___  
**When demand exceeds ceiling:** Pause intake / Extend lead time / Price adjustment / Controlled overflow (pre-approved)

Mara stared at the paper like it was an insult.

Lauren picked it up and read it in silence.

Sarah leaned over Lauren’s shoulder. “You know if we write ‘controlled overflow’ in a document, the universe will punish us.”

Celeste’s eyes narrowed. “Sarah.”

Sarah held up her hands. “I’m helping.”

Charlotte stepped closer and looked down at the page, thoughtful. “Buffer requirement is important.”

Mara’s gaze flicked to Charlotte again—annoyed, but not dismissive. Mara respected correct thinking, even when she hated who it came from.

Celeste pointed at the line. “Exactly. We currently use our buffer as spare time. Which means we don’t have buffer. We have denial.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “Accurate.”

Mara’s voice was sharp. “Enough. What number.”

Celeste met her eyes. This was the hinge.

She could have fought for an aggressive figure to prove competence. She could have “won” by pushing the ceiling high. That would be the old impulse: prove you can carry everything.

Instead she chose the mature number. The one that protected the work.

“We set it at what we can do correctly with one bad day per week,” Celeste said. “Because people have bad days. Because bodies fail. Because life happens. We plan for it, or it plans for us.”

Silence again.

Lauren nodded once. “That’s the right model.”

Sarah tilted her head. “One bad day a week seems… generous.”

Celeste looked at her. “It’s realistic.”

Sarah smiled. “Fair.”

Mara’s eyes were hard. “And if we exceed it.”

Celeste answered without hesitation. “Then we choose which lever to pull. Deliberately. Not in panic. Not as a confession. As policy.”

Mara stared at the paper for a long moment, jaw tight enough to crack.

Then she did something that, for Mara, counted as agreement.

She reached for the marker and wrote at the top of the page in brutal capital letters:

**CEILING IS A STANDARD.**

She shoved the sheet back toward Celeste.

“Fine,” Mara said. “Draft it properly. Bring it back tomorrow. And if anyone uses this as an excuse to do sloppy work, I’ll end them.”

Sarah lifted her coffee. “She’s so loving.”

Lauren’s voice was calm. “It’s not an excuse. It’s a safeguard.”

Mara’s eyes stayed on Lauren. “Good.”

Celeste felt her chest ease in a way she didn’t allow often.

They weren’t just surviving growth. They were governing it.

Charlotte’s hand brushed Celeste’s arm lightly as she passed behind her, a small grounding touch that said: *this is what it looks like when women build a life.*

Celeste closed the laptop.

“Okay,” she said, steady. “We have a ceiling.”

Sarah grinned. “And nobody died.”

Mara’s voice was flat. “Yet.”

Lauren capped her pen. “Next meeting: overflow criteria.”

Sarah groaned. “I hate all of you.”

Celeste looked around the table—her women—and felt something settle into place.

Not romance.

Structure.

The kind that held when everything else got heavy.

**Robyn:**

Scene 2 — “Near-Miss”

**Emily:**

## Scene 2 — “Near-Miss” (Lauren POV, past tense)

Lauren didn’t like surprises.

Not because she was rigid. Because surprises were where people got hurt—financially, physically, reputationally. At Wardrobe, surprises also meant seams failing in public, which was its own kind of injury.

She had the ledger open on the cutting table, not for drama, for tracking. Batch numbers. Courier times. The new ceiling draft clipped beside it. A pencil behind her ear, like she was about to become the kind of woman she’d once mocked.

Mara was at the pressing station, shoulders squared, working fast. Too fast.

Lauren clocked it the way she clocked everything. Speed without conversation. Silence that had an edge. A kind of tightness around Mara’s mouth that meant pain or anger, and with Mara those often wore the same face.

Sarah drifted past with a stack of dispatch slips and murmured, “She’s in a mood.”

Lauren didn’t look up. “She’s in something.”

Sarah paused. “You’re not going to say it.”

“No,” Lauren said. “I’m going to measure it.”

Charlotte was in the fitting space, half-dressed in a test garment, pinning and unpinning with patient precision. It was one of the newer lines—“nymph” adjacent but altered, Mara’s new cuts accommodating the subtle shift in Charlotte’s shape without ever calling it by name. The fabric looked expensive in the way good fabric did: quiet about it.

Lauren watched Charlotte tug once at a seam, frown, then mark a spot with chalk.

“Movement stress,” Charlotte called out, calm. “Left side seam. When I reach.”

Mara didn’t answer.

That was another tell. Mara normally replied to data like it was oxygen.

“Mara,” Lauren said, not loud.

Mara’s voice came back sharp from the pressing station. “I heard.”

Lauren held her gaze a beat. “Then respond.”

Mara’s eyes flashed. “Not everything needs a conversation.”

Lauren’s mouth tightened. “At this scale, yes, it does.”

Sarah made a soft sound like she was swallowing a laugh and thought better of it.

Mara turned away as if she hadn’t heard.

Lauren watched her press another panel, movements precise but slightly… clipped. Like she was forcing her hands through glue.

Lauren made a note on the dispatch slip. Nothing dramatic. Just a small line: **MARA—FAST / SILENT**.

A courier pickup was scheduled for three. It was already two-twenty.

Lauren went to the dispatch rack and checked the hanging garments one by one, fingers running along seams the way her eyes did along contracts. The new ceiling policy was only ink on paper so far. Until it became behaviour, she treated every shipment as a potential failure point.

She reached the last item in the batch: a period bodice with a clean line and a neat finish, tagged, wrapped, ready.

Something in Lauren’s body tightened.

It wasn’t visible at first glance. That was the problem.

Lauren lifted it off the rack and held it up in the light. Turned it slightly. Let the fabric fall the way it would on a body, not on a hanger.

Then she saw it: a faint distortion at the side seam. Not a tear. Not a gap. A minute tension line where there shouldn’t be one.

A stress point.

Lauren pinched the seam allowance between her fingers and pulled gently.

The stitches held.

She pulled again, a fraction more.

The fabric gave in the smallest way, like it was sighing.

Lauren’s stomach dropped.

It wasn’t a catastrophic failure. It was worse.

It was the kind of failure that would happen after an hour of wear, under the lights, in front of an audience, when someone moved and trusted the garment.

Lauren carried it to the fitting space.

“Charlotte,” she said.

Charlotte stepped out, took the bodice without comment, and tested it the way she always did—methodical, not panicked. She hooked her thumb under the seam and applied pressure at the movement point.

The seam didn’t rip.

But the fabric shifted again, almost imperceptibly.

Charlotte’s eyes narrowed. “That’s not correct.”

Lauren’s jaw tightened. “No.”

Charlotte looked at the tag. “This is in the three o’clock batch.”

Lauren nodded. “Yes.”

Charlotte didn’t ask who made it. She didn’t need to. Wardrobe wasn’t large enough for anonymity.

But she did something Lauren respected deeply: she didn’t make it personal. She made it procedural.

“Hold shipment,” Charlotte said simply. “Log as near-miss. Find root.”

Lauren felt relief—sharp and immediate—because the culture was holding. It was behaving the way they’d said it would.

Lauren walked back toward the pressing station with the bodice draped over her arm like evidence.

Mara was still pressing, face set, jaw clenched, and when she looked up her eyes were bright in a way that made Lauren’s chest tighten: too much pain, too much stubbornness.

Lauren stopped in front of her.

“Mara,” she said, calm.

Mara’s gaze flicked to the garment. “What.”

Lauren held it up, turned it so Mara could see the distortion line.

“This almost shipped,” Lauren said.

Mara’s eyes sharpened. She snatched it, ran her fingers down the seam like she could erase the flaw by touch.

Her face changed—one beat of recognition, then fury.

At the garment. At the day. At time itself.

“You tugged it,” Mara snapped, as if Lauren had bullied the fabric into failing.

Lauren didn’t flinch. “I tested it.”

Mara’s jaw flexed. “It would have held.”

Lauren’s voice stayed level. “It would have failed in wear.”

Silence.

Mara stared at the seam, then at Lauren, and Lauren saw the fear under the anger: the horror of being wrong, the horror of being human, the horror of being the reason Wardrobe broke its own promise.

Sarah had drifted closer and now stood in the doorway, coffee forgotten.

“Well,” Sarah said lightly, “this is fun.”

Lauren shot her a look. Sarah shut up.

Mara’s fingers tightened around the bodice until her knuckles went pale.

“It doesn’t leave,” Mara said flatly.

“I know,” Lauren replied.

Mara moved to the table with the ledger and wrote with aggressive precision, pen scratching hard.

**FAILURE POINT:** side seam pull at movement stress  
**CAUSE:** insufficient reinforcement at seam allowance / stitch tension  
**ACTION:** rework + retest

She wrote like she was punishing the page.

Lauren watched, then spoke carefully. “We need to know why it happened.”

Mara didn’t look up. “It happened because the fabric’s fussy.”

Lauren’s voice stayed calm. “It happened because someone was tired.”

Mara’s pen stopped.

Her head lifted slowly.

“Don’t,” Mara said, cold.

Lauren held her gaze. “I’m not accusing you. I’m naming risk.”

Mara’s eyes flashed. “You think I did sloppy work.”

Lauren’s chest tightened because she could hear the shame trying to twist itself into anger.

“No,” Lauren said. “I think you’re in pain and forcing your body through it. And that changes outcomes.”

Mara’s mouth opened as if to bite back.

Then she closed it. Her nostrils flared. She looked away like looking at Lauren too long might crack something open.

Charlotte appeared behind Lauren, quiet, holding chalk and a scrap of pattern paper.

“Reinforcement here,” Charlotte said, simply, and handed Mara the note like a technician handing another technician a solution.

Mara took it without looking at Charlotte.

Sarah leaned against the doorframe and, for once, kept her humour in its cage. When she spoke her voice was low, careful.

“Near-miss is… good,” Sarah said. “It means we caught it.”

Mara’s jaw tightened. “It means we almost didn’t.”

Lauren nodded once. “Yes.”

Mara stared at the ledger as if it had betrayed her.

Lauren made herself speak the line that mattered, the one that would decide what happened next.

“We hold the batch,” Lauren said. “We fix it. We retest. We log it.”

Mara’s voice went hard. “We don’t have time.”

Lauren didn’t blink. “Then we don’t ship.”

Silence.

Mara lifted her head, eyes sharp as a blade. “You would turn away revenue.”

Lauren’s answer was immediate. “I would turn away a public failure.”

Mara stared at her.

Then she looked down at Charlotte’s note, at the reinforcement mark, at the seam that had almost humiliated them.

She exhaled once, through her nose, like anger was the only way she could breathe.

“Fine,” Mara said. “Fix it.”

Lauren nodded. “Now.”

Mara’s eyes flicked toward the pressing station, toward the pile of work, toward the time.

Then, like a woman swallowing something bitter, she said, “Sarah. Call the courier. Push pickup.”

Sarah blinked. “Me?”

“Yes,” Mara snapped. “Use your charm for something useful.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Yes, ma’am.”

She disappeared toward the front, already pulling out her phone.

Lauren watched Mara pick up the bodice again, hands steadying now that they had a plan, the fury redirected into craft.

Charlotte stayed at Lauren’s shoulder, quiet.

“You were right,” Charlotte murmured, so only Lauren could hear. “About measuring.”

Lauren swallowed, throat tight. “We measure so we don’t lie.”

Charlotte nodded once. “And so we don’t blame.”

Lauren looked at her—this woman who had learned to speak like policy without losing softness—and felt something in her chest ease.

She turned back to Mara.

“Mara,” Lauren said, calm. “After we fix this, we talk.”

Mara didn’t look up. “About what.”

Lauren held her gaze anyway. “About load.”

Mara’s mouth tightened. “I’m fine.”

Lauren’s voice stayed even. “That’s not an answer.”

Mara’s hands paused for the briefest second.

Then she returned to work.

But she didn’t tell Lauren to get out.

And in Wardrobe language, that counted as the first crack in the wall.

**Robyn:**

Scene 3 — “Two Signatures”

**Emily:**

## Scene 3 — “Two Signatures” (Lauren POV, past tense)

They fixed the bodice. Retested it. Logged it.

They didn’t talk about it while their hands were busy, because Wardrobe wasn’t sentimental and panic never improved stitching. But Lauren watched Mara all afternoon—how she held herself, how she blinked more than usual, how she kept her shoulders squared like posture could override biology.

By five, the courier had gone. The floor had been swept. The room had that end-of-day hush that made even the machines feel tired.

Mara remained at the pressing station as if leaving would admit something.

Lauren set the ledger on the main table, opened it to the near-miss entry, and placed a fresh sheet beside it: the draft she and Celeste had been building since the ceiling meeting.

Sarah hovered by the doorway with her tote half-packed, lingering the way she did when she knew something interesting was about to happen.

Charlotte stood near the fitting room curtain, folding a test garment with quiet care—present, but refusing to take up unnecessary space.

Celeste arrived from the front with her laptop under her arm, eyes sharp, hair slightly disordered in the way it got when she’d been thinking hard.

“Okay,” Celeste said. “Now.”

Mara’s head lifted. “Now what.”

Celeste set the laptop down without ceremony. “Governance.”

Mara’s mouth tightened. “We have governance. I decide.”

Lauren didn’t look away. “That’s not governance. That’s a bottleneck.”

Mara’s eyes flashed, but she didn’t speak.

Celeste clicked the draft open on her screen and turned it so they could see. No colours, no corporate gloss. Just black text and clarity.

**SHIPMENT SIGN-OFF — PROPOSED RULE**  
**All outbound shipments require two signatories:**  
1) **Mara** (design/standards) **or** delegated design authority  
2) **Operations** (Lauren) **or** delegated ops authority  
**Exceptions:** must be logged + approved by both signatories.  
**Purpose:** reduce single-point failure risk; protect standards under scaling and fatigue.

Mara stared at it like it was a personal insult.

“This,” Mara said, voice flat with disgust, “is bureaucracy.”

Celeste didn’t flinch. “This is continuity.”

“It slows us down,” Mara snapped.

“It saves us from public failure,” Lauren replied.

Mara’s gaze cut to Lauren. “I don’t make public failures.”

Lauren held her eyes, calm. “Today almost did.”

Silence snapped into place.

Sarah made a small noise—half breath, half restrained comment—then wisely said nothing.

Mara’s jaw flexed. “That was fabric.”

Lauren shook her head once. “That was load.”

Mara looked away as if the word itself was an accusation.

Celeste kept her tone steady. “Mara, I’m not taking your authority. I’m distributing responsibility so the work survives your bad days.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “My bad days are not the business’s concern.”

Lauren’s mouth tightened. “Yes, they are. Because you’re in the system.”

Mara’s gaze sharpened. “I built the system.”

Lauren nodded. “Exactly. Which is why it can survive.”

Mara’s expression flickered—annoyance, fear, pride, all braided together and denied.

She turned slightly toward Charlotte, as if to redirect the threat. “And what. We add more people who don’t know standards.”

Charlotte didn’t flinch. She didn’t defend herself. She just offered truth.

“Standards can be taught,” Charlotte said calmly. “If they’re written.”

Mara stared at her.

Charlotte’s voice stayed steady. “If they’re only in your head, they die with your stamina.”

The sentence hit like a scalpel.

Sarah, unable to resist, murmured, “Oof.”

Mara’s eyes flashed. “Enough.”

Celeste’s voice cut in cleanly. “No. Not enough. This is the point.”

Mara glared at her. “You think you can run this with a policy.”

Celeste met her gaze. “We already do. The no-filming rule was a policy. The fitting-space lock was a policy. The courier routine is a policy. This is the same thing—just internal.”

Mara’s mouth tightened. “Those are boundaries. This is—”

“Protection,” Lauren said. She tapped the ledger open on the table. “It protects the work. And it protects you, even if you refuse to admit you need it.”

Mara’s eyes hardened. “Don’t.”

Lauren didn’t soften. “We’re not doing heroics anymore.”

Mara’s lip curled. “Heroics built this place.”

Lauren’s tone stayed even, colder than usual. “Heroics nearly broke it.”

Silence.

Mara looked from Lauren to Celeste to Sarah to Charlotte, as if she’d woken up and found a team where she’d expected subordinates.

Sarah lifted her hands slightly, mock innocent. “Just for the record, I’d like to say I am extremely subordinate.”

Lauren didn’t look at her. “Not now.”

Sarah shut her mouth, chastened—proof the culture held.

Celeste stepped forward, calm, and added the line Lauren knew Mara could hear.

“A standard that depends on one person isn’t a standard,” Celeste said. “It’s a risk.”

Mara’s nostrils flared.

“I’m not a risk,” she said, low.

Lauren’s voice softened by a fraction—not sentimental, just real. “You’re human.”

Mara’s face went hard again. “I don’t have time to be human.”

Sarah’s voice, unexpectedly quiet, slid into the space. “Well, your body does.”

Mara shot her a look that could have turned sand to glass.

Sarah held it, unflinching now, because she knew the humour was over. “I’m not being cute,” Sarah said. “I’m being accurate.”

Mara looked away.

Lauren slid the paper across the table. “We don’t need your feelings. We need your agreement.”

Mara’s eyes snapped back. “You don’t get to talk to me like that.”

Lauren met her gaze. “I do, because this is governance. Not hierarchy.”

Celeste added, matter-of-fact, “And because without it, we’re gambling with the thing you care about most.”

Mara stared at the sheet.

The room held its breath.

Finally, Mara said, coldly, “Who’s the second signature.”

Lauren didn’t hesitate. “Me.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “You’ll slow it down.”

Lauren’s voice stayed even. “I’ll stop it leaving if it’s wrong.”

Mara stared at her. Then she said, sharp, “And if you’re wrong.”

Lauren’s answer was immediate. “Then you correct me, and we log the correction, and the system improves.”

Mara’s jaw tightened. She hated the logic because it was hers, reflected back.

Celeste added, “And I’ll sign on contracts and external commitments. I’m not signing things I can’t ship.”

Mara’s gaze flicked to Celeste. “Fine.”

Sarah lifted a hand. “What about me. I’d like to sign something. For the experience.”

Lauren’s gaze finally cut to her. “Client-facing exceptions.”

Sarah blinked. “Oh. Actual responsibility.”

“Yes,” Lauren said.

Sarah’s mouth curved. “Delicious.”

Charlotte spoke softly from the side. “And I can be secondary sign-off for wear-test items. When needed.”

Mara’s eyes flicked to Charlotte again. She looked as if she wanted to refuse on reflex.

Then she didn’t.

She looked down at the ledger, reached for her pen, and opened to a blank page.

Mara wrote in brutal, neat capitals:

**SHIP ONLY WITH TWO SIGN-OFFS.**

She underlined it once, hard enough to dent the paper.

She shoved the ledger toward Lauren like she was surrendering nothing.

“Sign,” Mara said.

Lauren picked up the pen and signed beneath Mara’s line.

Her signature looked ordinary on the page. The effect wasn’t.

The power of it wasn’t symbolic. It was structural.

Celeste watched and felt something settle. This was what growing up looked like for a business: replacing adrenaline with systems.

Sarah let out a small breath she’d been holding and murmured, almost gently, “Look at us. Proper little adults.”

Mara’s head snapped up. “Do not.”

Sarah lifted her hands, smiling. “I’m not being sentimental. I’m being proud.”

Mara glared at her, but the heat had drained out of it.

Lauren capped the pen and closed the ledger.

Mara’s voice was low. “Don’t make it a big thing.”

Lauren met her eyes and let her voice drop by a fraction.

“We won’t,” Lauren said. “We’ll just do it right.”

Mara stared at her for a beat longer, then turned back to the pressing station as if she could end the meeting by returning to work.

But she didn’t take the rule back.

And that, in Wardrobe, was how you knew a boundary had become policy.

**Robyn:**

Scene 4 — “The Slip”

**Emily:**

## Scene 4 — “The Slip” (Celeste POV, past tense)

It wasn’t dramatic.

That was the problem.

If Mara had collapsed, if she’d made a sound, if she’d given them a clean emergency to respond to, Wardrobe would have snapped into action without friction.

But Mara didn’t collapse.

Mara *slowed*.

It was a Tuesday that should have been ordinary—orders in the pipeline, the new ceiling draft taped to the wall near the ledger, the Two-Signature rule already behaving like it had always existed.

The room was bright with summer light. Too bright. Celeste had noticed it earlier—the way Mara’s eyes narrowed at the windows as if sunlight were an insult.

Mara was at the cutting table, pattern weights set, shears in hand, jaw tight. She worked with that familiar, ruthless precision, as if speed could prove she hadn’t been changed by anything.

Celeste was on the phone at the front, confirming a supplier’s minimum order and arguing, politely, for better lead times. Lauren was in the back with the roster, setting up the week like a small, quiet act of war against chaos. Sarah was at the doorway dealing with a customer enquiry with her best terrifying politeness.

Charlotte was near the fitting curtain, wear-testing a sleeve movement range, chalk marking a stress point and then wiping her hands clean like she was closing a loop.

Everything was fine.

Until it wasn’t.

Mara’s shears paused mid-cut.

Not for a choice. For a fraction of a second too long.

Celeste looked up from her call, instinct catching the hitch in rhythm the way you caught a wrong note in music.

Mara’s left hand went flat on the table. Her shoulders stayed squared. Her face stayed composed.

But her eyes unfocused for half a beat, as if the room had shifted its edges.

Sarah, mid-sentence at the doorway, stopped talking. She watched Mara the way Sarah watched a threat.

Lauren looked up from her roster as if she’d felt something move.

Charlotte went still.

Mara blinked once. Twice.

Then she reached for the shears again.

Lauren was already walking toward her.

“Mara,” Lauren said, calm.

Mara’s voice was clipped. “I’m fine.”

Celeste heard it in the tone: not reassurance, not truth—command.

Lauren didn’t accept it. “Stop for thirty seconds.”

Mara’s eyes flashed. “No.”

Celeste ended her call without thinking—one sentence to the supplier, polite and final—and crossed the room.

Mara’s fingers tightened on the shears.

Then she lowered them to the table, slowly, like setting down a weapon she resented needing.

Her other hand remained splayed flat on the wood. She wasn’t shaking. She wasn’t fainting.

She was simply… braced.

Sarah stepped closer, voice unusually quiet. “Head?”

Mara didn’t look at her. “Light.”

Lauren’s jaw tightened. “Migraine.”

Mara’s mouth curled with contempt. “Don’t diagnose me.”

Lauren’s voice stayed even. “I’m naming risk.”

Mara took a breath through her nose, controlled, but it didn’t fully steady her. She blinked again, hard, like she was trying to clear something from her vision.

Charlotte moved without being asked. She crossed to the window and pulled the blind down halfway, cutting the glare. Then she dimmed the overhead light nearest the cutting table with the small efficiency of someone who’d learned that comfort was a legitimate goal.

The room softened.

Mara’s shoulders loosened by a fraction, and the fact that it helped made her expression tighten with fury.

“I said I’m fine,” Mara repeated, sharper now.

Sarah didn’t joke.

That alone made the moment real.

“No,” Sarah said, voice low, “you’re functioning. That’s not the same thing.”

Mara’s eyes snapped to her. “Don’t you start.”

Lauren set a glass of water on the table as if it were an instruction. “Sit.”

Mara didn’t move.

Lauren didn’t raise her voice, but the authority in it was unmistakable. “Sit. Or I’ll call it. Your choice.”

Mara stared at her as if she couldn’t believe she was being handled in her own studio.

“You don’t get to—” Mara began.

Celeste cut in, calm as a ledger entry. “We do.”

Mara’s eyes flashed to Celeste.

Celeste’s tone stayed measured. “Because Two Signatures means no single point of failure. And right now you are trying to make your body a single point of failure.”

Mara’s jaw flexed.

She hated being framed.

She hated being correct.

Lauren pulled a stool over with one foot and placed it behind Mara’s knees like she was closing a gate. “Sit.”

Mara’s hand hovered over the table as if she might grip it forever.

Then, with a visible effort, she lowered herself onto the stool.

It wasn’t surrender.

It was physics winning.

For a moment she sat perfectly upright, chin lifted, as if posture could make the symptom disappear.

Sarah crouched slightly so she wasn’t towering. “Do you have meds.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “No.”

Lauren’s pen appeared in her hand without anyone seeing her pick it up. “Okay. We’re logging.”

Mara’s head snapped up. “Do not.”

Lauren didn’t blink. “Near-miss taught us we don’t do secrets.”

Mara’s mouth tightened. “This is not the same.”

Lauren’s voice stayed calm. “It is exactly the same. It’s risk. And it affects output.”

Charlotte spoke quietly from the side, not emotional, just precise. “And quality.”

Mara’s gaze flicked to Charlotte with a flash of irritation.

Charlotte held it without flinching. “You taught us that.”

Silence.

Mara looked away first.

Celeste took a breath and did what she’d been learning to do: not soothe, not dramatise—structure.

“Okay,” Celeste said. “Here is what we do.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “We keep working.”

Celeste ignored that. “We redistribute.”

She looked at Lauren. “Ops takes dispatch and supplier calls. No exceptions.”

Lauren nodded once. “Done.”

Celeste looked at Sarah. “Front-of-house. No Mara contact load today.”

Sarah’s jaw set. “Gladly.”

Celeste looked at Charlotte. “Wear-test and retest queue. You’ve got it?”

Charlotte nodded. “Yes.”

Celeste looked at Mara, steady. “And you stop cutting.”

Mara’s eyes hardened. “No.”

Celeste didn’t argue. She offered the correct lever.

“This isn’t negotiation,” Celeste said. “It’s governance.”

Mara’s voice went cold. “You’re enjoying this.”

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted. “No. I’m preventing you from turning your pride into a defect.”

Mara’s nostrils flared.

Lauren spoke, and her voice was quiet enough that it carried. “Mara. We’re not your employees right now.”

Mara’s eyes flicked to her.

Lauren finished, flat and absolute: “We’re your structure.”

Sarah added, very softly, the line that made Mara freeze because it sounded like policy, not pity.

“We’re not asking,” Sarah said. “We’re staffing.”

Mara’s jaw tightened like she might refuse on principle.

Then she blinked again, slower this time, and Celeste saw a brief shadow of something under the anger: fatigue. Fear. The horror of not being able to command her own body.

Mara’s voice came out lower. “I don’t have time for this.”

Lauren’s answer was immediate. “You don’t have time to ignore it.”

Charlotte stepped closer—not to comfort, not to plead. To make it easier for Mara to accept without losing face.

“We can hold the line,” Charlotte said, simply. “For today.”

Mara’s gaze flicked to her, sharp.

Charlotte didn’t soften. “And we can still ship correctly.”

The words mattered. Mara cared about correctness more than she cared about being cared for.

Celeste watched Mara’s shoulders drop a fraction, as if the decision had been made by something deeper than pride.

Mara spoke through clenched teeth. “Fine.”

Lauren’s pen scratched across her notebook, not dramatic, just factual: **Mara symptomatic—light sensitivity; ceased cutting; redistributed load; revisit scheduling.**

Mara heard the pen and glared. “Don’t.”

Lauren didn’t stop writing. “We don’t do secrets.”

Mara’s voice was flat. “You’re making it a big thing.”

Celeste shook her head. “We’re making it a small thing, on purpose, before it becomes a big thing.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “And what. You’ll put me on a roster like a trainee.”

Lauren capped her pen. “We’ll put you on a roster like the most critical component you are.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Listen to Mum, Mara.”

Mara shot her a look.

Sarah held it, unflinching. “I’m serious.”

Celeste slid a single sheet across the table—fresh paper, clean text, already drafted because she’d been thinking about this since the near-miss.

**MARA LOAD RULE — DRAFT**  
- No late nights alone  
- One non-production day per week (research / design review / painting)  
- Design sign-off windows (no ad hoc approvals under fatigue)  
- Two signatures enforced without exception  
- If symptoms recur: immediate redistribution + logged

Mara stared at it like it was a threat.

Lauren watched her, steady.

Charlotte stayed quiet, letting Mara have dignity.

Sarah, for once, didn’t make a joke.

Mara’s jaw tightened. “Painting.”

Celeste’s tone stayed mild. “Yes. Your brain needs an outlet that isn’t pressure.”

Mara’s eyes flicked up, suspicious. “You’re all conspiring.”

Lauren didn’t deny it. “Yes.”

Mara looked around the room—at the women who had built themselves into a team without asking for her permission to be one.

For a moment, Celeste thought she might explode.

Instead Mara said, rough and reluctant, “Fine.”

Then, immediately, as if she could claw back power with contempt: “But don’t get sentimental.”

Sarah’s smile returned—small, controlled, respectful.

“Wouldn’t dream of it,” Sarah said.

Lauren picked up the rule sheet and clipped it to the ledger like it had always belonged there.

Charlotte adjusted the blind again, lowering it just enough to soften the light further, and Mara—still rigid, still furious—didn’t tell her to stop.

Celeste stood for a moment, letting the room settle into its new shape.

Then she turned and went back to her laptop, already drafting the formal version of the rule, because that was the real love language of Wardrobe:

Not speeches.

Not rescue.

A system that held.

And in the corner of her eye, she saw Mara sit on the stool, eyes closed for a beat, allowing—just barely—what the rest of them had already decided:

That Wardrobe could protect everyone.

Including Mara.

**Robyn:**

I have GOT to stop reading for a moment. This is EASILY the most powerful part of the story. All of their characters intertwine... they all shine in their special ways. And they *INCLUDE* Mara, despite herself. So so good, this, Emily.

**Emily:**

I’m really glad it hit the way it’s meant to hit — because *this* is the story’s spine showing itself: not romance-as-rescue, but **women building a structure that holds even the woman who taught them how.** Mara included, not indulged. Protected, not patronised.

If you do pause here (which is wise), this is also a beautiful “hinge” point in the larger arc. A few small, non-melodramatic aftershocks you can keep in your pocket for later, whenever you’re ready to move again:

- **Mara’s compliance looks like resentment + follow-through.** She doesn’t thank them. She simply… abides by the rule once. That’s massive for her.
- **Lauren becomes quietly terrifying as ops lead.** Not emotional, not harsh — just *immovable*.
- **Sarah’s growth is that she knows when humour stops being help.** The joke-girl becomes the culture guard.
- **Charlotte’s voice continues to mature: “for today” / “we can still ship correctly.”** That’s her belonging expressed as competence, not appeal.
- **Celeste’s leadership becomes adult: she doesn’t “win”; she institutes policy.** She proves she’s worthy of the MBA without turning into a corporate parody.

When you come back, we can either:
1) write the immediate “next morning” micro-scene (Mara arrives, sees the Load Rule clipped to the ledger, hates it, follows it), or  
2) jump to the first moment the system gets tested (a big order arrives and they *hold the ceiling*), or  
3) pivot into Celeste’s expanding external role — contracts, suppliers, and how she uses “Wardrobe voice” in those rooms.

For now: stop reading, breathe, and let it settle. This is the kind of scene that changes the whole book’s gravity.

**Robyn:**

## Scene 5 — “Front Door”

You offered an optional scene to allow Sarah's role to deepen. Would you be willing to explore that now?

**Emily:**

## Scene 5 — “Front Door” (Sarah POV, past tense)

Sarah had always been good at doors.

Not the literal hinges and locks—though she could do those too—but the social kind. The invisible threshold where someone decided they were entitled to more than they were being offered.

Wardrobe had learned the hard way that the front door wasn’t just an entry.

It was a filter.

And lately, the world had been pressing its face against the glass.

It was mid-morning, the light still soft, the studio already warm with the hum of work. Lauren was in the back with the roster and the dispatch stack, moving through tasks like a metronome. Celeste was at the cutting table with her laptop open, on speaker with a supplier, voice calm and exact. Mara was seated—not standing—at the end of the main table, sketching with grim concentration, the blinds half-drawn to keep the glare off her eyes.

The Load Rule sat clipped to the ledger like a dare.

Mara hadn’t commented on it today.

That, Sarah had learned, was how you knew it was working.

Charlotte was in the fitting space, pinning a test seam with the patience of someone who’d stopped apologising for taking time.

Sarah was at the front, folding tissue paper into the kind of clean squares that made customers think you cared—which, inconveniently, Wardrobe did.

The bell above the door chimed.

Sarah looked up.

A man stepped in.

Not a courier. Not a lost tourist. Not a husband hovering at the threshold with a sheepish smile. This one walked in like he expected the space to rearrange itself around him.

Mid-thirties. Gym-fit. Phone in hand already, screen up, thumb poised. The expression on his face wasn’t hostile. It was worse.

Curious.

“Hi,” he said, bright. “Is this Wardrobe?”

Sarah gave him her friendliest smile.

It was the kind that didn’t contain an invitation.

“It is,” she said. “We’re not open to walk-ins.”

He laughed lightly, as if she’d told a charming joke. “Oh, I won’t take long. I just—” he lifted his phone, “—saw you guys on TikTok and I’m in the area. Thought I’d pop in. It’s, like, iconic.”

Sarah kept her face pleasant.

She could feel Lauren’s attention shift in the back, like a weight redistributed. Could feel Celeste pause midsentence on the phone. Could feel the whole studio’s energy tighten the way fabric did before it tore.

Sarah didn’t look back.

The door was her job.

“I understand,” Sarah said. “But we’re private. No filming. No visitors.”

He smiled wider, still cheerful. “I’m not filming. I mean—” his thumb moved on the screen, betraying him, “—not yet. I just want to see the setup. Like, the vibe.”

Sarah’s smile didn’t change.

Inside her, something settled. A clean decision.

“No,” she said.

He blinked. “Sorry?”

“No,” Sarah repeated, same tone, same volume. Not emotional. Just final. “You can’t come in.”

He glanced past her shoulder, trying to look around her body as if she were furniture. “I’m not trying to be weird. I’m a fan. You’re a business. Don’t you want—”

“Exposure?” Sarah supplied, still smiling. “No.”

The word landed harder than it should have, because it was so calmly delivered.

The man’s smile faltered. “That’s… odd. I mean, it’s a public place.”

Sarah’s eyes held his, bright and unfriendly in a polite way. “It’s a workplace.”

He laughed again, a little higher, the laugh of someone used to women smoothing things over for him. “Okay, okay. Chill. I’m just looking.”

Sarah’s smile sharpened.

“This is not a gallery,” she said. “This is not theatre. This is not a behind-the-scenes tour. It’s women working. And you’re in the wrong place.”

The man’s cheeks flushed slightly. “You don’t have to be rude.”

Sarah tilted her head. “I’m not being rude. I’m being clear.”

Behind her, she heard the soft scrape of a chair.

Mara.

Sarah kept her eyes on the man, her body angled so he couldn’t see further into the studio.

He lifted his phone slightly as if to show it to her, as if proof of his intention would grant him access. “See? I’m not recording. I just want to meet—” his gaze flicked inward again, hungry, “—the team. Like, the designer lady.”

Sarah’s smile softened into something almost pitying.

“We’re not doing that,” she said.

The man frowned. “Why.”

Sarah let the word hang for a beat, then answered with a sentence that was as old as the world and just as necessary.

“Because you don’t get to,” she said.

He stared at her.

Then, because entitlement didn’t like being blocked by a woman with an accent and a smile, he tried to step around her.

Sarah moved one foot, subtle, shifting her stance so the doorway stayed occupied.

It wasn’t aggressive.

It was competent.

“Stop,” Sarah said, still pleasant.

He froze. “Are you serious?”

Sarah’s voice dropped slightly, not in threat, in clarity. “Yes.”

In the back, Lauren’s footsteps sounded—approaching, unhurried, calm. Not rushing, because rushing made it a scene, and the goal was always to avoid scenes.

The man noticed Lauren and visibly brightened, as if an authority had arrived he could negotiate with.

“Oh, hi,” he said, aiming his smile at Lauren. “I’m just—”

Lauren didn’t return the smile.

She didn’t scowl either.

She simply looked at him the way you looked at a risk on a spreadsheet: unemotional, accurate.

“We’re private,” Lauren said. “You need to leave.”

The man blinked. “I’m not doing anything.”

Lauren’s gaze stayed level. “You are refusing a boundary. That’s doing something.”

He scoffed. “This is ridiculous. It’s a business.”

Sarah’s smile returned, bright as sunlight and twice as cold. “Exactly. Businesses have policies.”

He tried a different angle. “Can I at least get a quick photo with the sign? Outside? For my followers? It’s free marketing—”

“No,” Sarah said again.

Lauren added, calm and precise, “And if you post our location, we’ll take action. Do you understand.”

That finally landed. Not because he respected them—because he respected consequences.

His face hardened. “Wow. Feminists, huh.”

Sarah’s smile widened, and for a brief second, she looked almost delighted.

“Correct,” she said. “And we’re practising.”

The man opened his mouth, then closed it.

He took one step backward toward the door, eyes flicking around as if searching for someone to validate his outrage.

No one did.

Mara hadn’t stood. She hadn’t needed to. She sat at the table, eyes on her sketch, silent and immovable, like the studio itself had decided he wasn’t worth her gaze.

Charlotte didn’t look up from her pinning.

Celeste continued her supplier call in the back, voice steady, as if this were so routine it didn’t merit a pause.

Which, Sarah realised, was the whole point.

Wardrobe wasn’t a place that became chaotic when a man showed up.

It was a place that stayed itself.

The man backed out, muttering, “Whatever,” and the bell chimed again as the door closed behind him.

Sarah walked to the lock and turned it, the little click loud in the sudden quiet.

She exhaled once.

Lauren looked at her, and there was something like approval in her eyes.

“Good,” Lauren said.

Sarah shrugged, as if this were nothing. “He wanted a vibe.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “And you gave him one.”

Sarah’s smile flashed. “I’m the vibe.”

From the table, Mara spoke without looking up.

“Next time,” Mara said, flat, “don’t let him get past the threshold.”

Sarah turned her head, grin sharp. “He didn’t.”

Mara’s pen paused for the briefest beat.

Then, still not looking up, Mara said, rougher than she intended, “Good.”

Sarah’s grin softened just slightly.

It wasn’t praise, not in Mara language.

It was acknowledgement.

Sarah went back to the tissue paper, hands steady, heart beating a fraction faster than she liked.

Because it had been nothing.

Because it had been everything.

Because the culture had held without ceremony.

And because, for once, Sarah hadn’t used humour to make something bearable.

She’d used authority to make it stop.

**Robyn:**

Charlotte and Celeste are at a café in the city. A well-dressed woman walks by. Everything about her - her attire, her perfume, her makeup, her smile - spoke of self-confidence, well-being, none of it ostentatious, all of working together to evoke a sense of wonder and in Charlotte, a new sensation: envy. "I'd like to be like that. I'd like to have that... presence." The woman's command over her femininity, how she artfully yet effortlessly let it flow from her had men straightening their ties. But that wasn't what Charlotte wanted? She didn't want to be attractive to anyone but Celeste. But she wanted that aura, that energy. She looked at Celeste anew. Celeste was like that. "Tell me, Celeste... how does one become... properly feminine? And that woman... and you: she was clearly enjoying it. You enjoy it, don't you?"

**Emily:**

They were at one of those city cafés that pretended it hadn’t made choices—white tile, quiet jazz, sunlight held at the window like a polite guest. Celeste had her flat white and her laptop closed for once. Charlotte had tea and both hands around the cup as if warmth were a kind of proof.

The woman passed in the slow lane between tables.

Not a spectacle. Not a performance. Just… coherence.

A tailored coat that didn’t shout money, only fit. Hair done with the kind of effort that looked like none. Makeup that didn’t announce itself, only refined what was already there. A scent that arrived after she did—soft, clean, faintly floral—like punctuation. The smile she gave the barista wasn’t flirtation. It was social fluency.

Men didn’t gape. That wasn’t it.

They *adjusted*. Straightened ties, shifted their posture, became more careful in their own skin as she moved by. Not because she demanded attention. Because her presence quietly implied standards.

Charlotte watched her go.

Celeste saw it happen: the change in Charlotte’s eyes. Not longing, not hunger—something else. A new and unfamiliar tightness.

Envy.

Charlotte’s gaze stayed on the woman until she disappeared into the street light, and then Charlotte looked back at Celeste as if seeing her properly for the first time.

“Tell me,” Charlotte said, quietly, “how does one become… properly feminine?”

Celeste didn’t answer immediately.

Because there were a dozen bad answers. Answers that would turn Charlotte into a project, a checklist, a costume. Answers that would make this about *appearing* instead of *being*.

Charlotte continued, as if she needed the words out in the open to see if they were safe.

“That woman—and you.” Charlotte’s eyes flicked toward the window, then returned. “She was clearly enjoying it. The way it… flowed. Not for them.” She nodded faintly at the men. “For herself.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, honest and cautious all at once. “I don’t want to be attractive to anyone but you. I’m not asking for… attention.”

Celeste leaned back slightly, studying her.

“What you want,” Celeste said, calm, “is not attention. It’s *authority.*”

Charlotte blinked, as if the word unlocked something.

Celeste went on, voice steady. “That woman has a kind of internal alignment. Everything on her is saying the same thing: I belong here. I know who I am. I’m not asking permission.”

Charlotte’s fingers tightened around her cup. “Yes.”

Celeste nodded once. “That’s the part you envied.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened. “And… how do I get it.”

Celeste smiled slightly, because Charlotte still expected femininity to be something you were granted if you did it correctly enough.

“You don’t ‘get’ it,” Celeste said. “You choose it. Repeatedly.”

Charlotte watched her, very still.

Celeste leaned in just a fraction—not to dominate, to be precise.

“Proper femininity,” Celeste said, “is not a look. It’s not even a behaviour. It’s an *orientation.*”

Charlotte’s brow furrowed. “Orientation.”

“Toward yourself,” Celeste said. “Not toward men. Not toward approval. Toward *self-respect.*”

Charlotte exhaled, slow. “That sounds… abstract.”

“It isn’t,” Celeste replied. “It’s practical. It’s a hundred small decisions that say: I’m worth care.”

Charlotte’s gaze dropped to her own hands, as if seeing all the small decisions she hadn’t been allowing herself to make.

Celeste’s voice softened slightly, but stayed firm. “And it’s not about becoming someone else. It’s about becoming *legible* as yourself.”

Charlotte looked up again, searching. “Legible how.”

Celeste’s answer was immediate.

“Fit,” she said. “Not sexy. Fit. Clothes that sit on your body like they were meant to. Not hiding. Not apologising. Not signalling ‘don’t look at me.’ Just… correct.”

Charlotte’s throat worked. “That feels dangerous.”

Celeste’s eyes held hers. “It feels dangerous because you were trained to think visibility equals vulnerability. It doesn’t. Visibility with boundaries is power.”

Charlotte went still. That landed.

“And then,” Celeste added, “posture. Not ‘dainty.’ Not performative. Just upright. Shoulders where they belong. Your body taking its space without making excuses.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened in a faint, thoughtful line. “And… the rest. The perfume. The makeup.”

Celeste smiled, small and knowing. “Tools.”

Charlotte blinked. “Tools.”

“Tools that can be used badly,” Celeste said. “To chase approval. To negotiate safety. To perform femininity like a job interview.”

Charlotte flinched slightly, because it was too accurate.

Celeste continued, unkind only to the lie. “Or tools that can be used well. To support your mood. To mark your day. To enjoy your own senses.”

Charlotte’s eyes stayed on hers. “You enjoy it.”

Celeste didn’t pretend she didn’t.

“Yes,” Celeste said simply. “I do.”

Charlotte’s face softened, uncertain. “Is it… wrong to want that.”

Celeste’s expression sharpened—not angry, just absolute.

“No,” Celeste said. “It’s human. And it’s female. And it’s yours if you want it.”

Charlotte swallowed. “But it’s easy to slip into… performance.”

Celeste nodded once. “So we set an intention.”

Charlotte waited.

Celeste held her gaze. “We do it for you. For your nervous system. For your sense of coherence. For the pleasure of being inside your own life.”

Charlotte’s fingers loosened around her cup. “And for you.”

Celeste’s smile returned—quick, private. “Yes. And for me.”

Charlotte looked out the window again, to where the woman had disappeared, then back.

“So what do I do,” Charlotte asked quietly, “first.”

Celeste didn’t give her a makeover. She gave her a standard.

“First,” Celeste said, “you pick one thing that makes you feel… slightly more like yourself. Not more ‘feminine.’ More *you.*”

Charlotte frowned slightly. “That could be anything.”

“Exactly,” Celeste said.

Charlotte thought for a long moment, then said, almost reluctantly, “Scent.”

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted. “Scent.”

Charlotte nodded once, as if committing to a decision. “It’s private. It’s for me. And… it changes how I feel in my skin.”

Celeste’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Charlotte hesitated, then added, quieter, “And for you.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Yes.”

Charlotte looked at her, earnest and steady. “Will you help me.”

Celeste didn’t answer like a girlfriend. She answered like the woman who ran Wardrobe.

“Yes,” Celeste said. “But I’m going to be strict.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “Strict how.”

Celeste leaned in slightly, voice low and certain. “We don’t buy a persona. We don’t buy insecurity. We don’t buy anything that makes you feel like you have to earn entry.”

Charlotte’s gaze held. “Okay.”

Celeste’s expression softened at the edges. “We buy one scent that feels like calm authority. Something you can wear under anything. Something that belongs to you.”

Charlotte exhaled, and the envy that had been sharp a moment ago shifted into something gentler—anticipation, maybe. Possibility.

Celeste reached across the table and covered Charlotte’s hand with her own—brief, steady, equal.

“And Charlotte,” Celeste added, voice quiet.

Charlotte looked up.

“That aura you want?” Celeste said. “It’s already in you. The work is letting yourself stop hiding it.”

Charlotte swallowed, then nodded once—an adult nod, not a grateful one.

“Good,” Charlotte said.

Celeste smiled. “Good.”

And outside, the city kept moving—full of women with presence, full of men adjusting their ties—while at their small table, Charlotte began to understand that femininity wasn’t a performance she had to perfect.

It was a life she was allowed to enjoy.

**Robyn:**

Scent - perfume - is about chemistry. Charlotte's chemistry has changed, of course. A drop in androgens will change the metabolism ... they go perfume shopping. Celeste's biggest challenge: Charlotte is showing signs of confusion, as if her person is beginning to fade again. She is so 'there' at Wardrobe, at home, everywhere but here in the perfume section in a large, expensive department store. Celeste senses her growing discomfort and stops. "We need to talk, don''t we?"

**Emily:**

They went on a weekday, because Celeste understood crowds the way she understood invoices: avoid them if you wanted a clean outcome.

The department store was all polished stone and soft lighting, arranged to make money feel like a natural state. The perfume floor was its own climate—cooler air, quieter voices, glass shelves gleaming with bottles like tiny, expensive trophies. Everything was curated to suggest that femininity could be purchased in measured sprays.

Charlotte stayed close at first, shoulders squared, eyes steady. She held her tote strap in one hand the way she did when she was keeping herself anchored.

Celeste watched her more than she watched the displays.

Because scent *was* chemistry. Charlotte was right about that. Her body had changed; her skin held warmth differently now, oils altered, a new softness at the edge of things. Perfume wasn’t just a bottle—on her, it would become something else.

They stopped at a counter that smelled like clean white linen and citrus peel—expensive without being sugary. The saleswoman drifted over in a black dress and a smile that didn’t intrude.

“Hi there,” she said. “Are you looking for something fresh? Something warm? Something—”

“Quiet,” Celeste said immediately, polite but firm. “Not loud. And not sweet.”

The saleswoman’s smile sharpened in professional approval. “Understood.”

She offered scent strips, arranged like invitations. Charlotte took one and held it with the careful seriousness she brought to fittings, as if doing it correctly mattered.

Celeste watched her inhale.

At first Charlotte nodded—small, thoughtful. Then her eyes shifted, not to the scent, but away, as if trying to step back from the sensory load.

The store was full of invisible hands: musks and vanillas and aldehydes reaching into the air, colliding and blooming. People walked past trailing clouds. The air was a conversation Charlotte hadn’t asked to join.

Charlotte’s mouth tightened.

Celeste saw the change the way she’d learned to see it in the early days: the slight withdrawal, the narrowing of presence, as if Charlotte were retreating to a smaller version of herself to stay safe.

Not panic. Not drama. Something quieter and more dangerous.

Disappearance.

Charlotte lifted another strip and sniffed.

Then she didn’t look at Celeste. She didn’t look at the bottles. She stared at nothing just beyond the counter, expression blank in a way that didn’t match the woman who could argue policy and set boundaries and swim.

The saleswoman offered a bottle.

Charlotte didn’t take it.

“Charlotte?” Celeste said softly.

Charlotte blinked, as if she hadn’t realised she’d left the room.

“I’m fine,” Charlotte murmured automatically.

Celeste felt a flare of alarm—controlled, immediate. She’d heard that phrase from Mara. From women trying to override biology with will.

It wasn’t the words that worried her. It was the cadence. The old cadence.

Charlotte’s fingers tightened on her tote strap until the knuckles went pale.

Celeste turned slightly, placing her body between Charlotte and the busiest part of the floor—not dramatic, just tactical. She gave the saleswoman a small, apologetic smile.

“We’re going to step aside for a moment,” Celeste said.

“Of course,” the woman replied instantly, with the grace of someone trained not to make it personal.

Celeste guided Charlotte two steps away—into a quieter corner near a display of scarves, where the air was still scented but less aggressive. She didn’t touch Charlotte’s back, didn’t steer her like a child. She simply moved, and let Charlotte choose to follow.

Charlotte did.

Celeste waited until Charlotte’s breathing was steady again. Then she spoke, low and direct.

“We need to talk, don’t we?”

Charlotte swallowed.

Her eyes lifted to Celeste’s, and Celeste saw the confusion in them—raw and unguarded. Not about perfume.

About being watched. About being exposed.

About being in a place where femininity was commodified and performed and policed all at once.

“Yes,” Charlotte said quietly. “I’m… fading.”

Celeste’s throat tightened, hot. She forced herself to keep her voice calm.

“Tell me what’s happening,” Celeste said.

Charlotte looked down at the scent strip still in her hand, as if it were evidence.

“It’s… too much,” Charlotte said. “Not the smell. The… premise.”

Celeste didn’t interrupt. She let Charlotte find the truth properly, rather than rushing in with reassurance.

Charlotte’s voice tightened. “At Wardrobe, it’s function. It’s craft. It’s standards. At home… it’s us.”

She swallowed again. “Here it feels like… femininity as performance. And I suddenly can’t tell which parts of me are real and which parts are—” she searched for the word, disgust flickering, “—a costume.”

Celeste felt something in her chest unclench and tighten again, because it was both painful and deeply intelligent.

“You’re not confused,” Celeste said softly. “You’re discerning.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked up. “It doesn’t feel like discernment. It feels like I’m disappearing.”

Celeste nodded once. “Because you’re in a context that used to erase you.”

Charlotte’s jaw tightened.

Celeste went on, precise. “This floor is designed to make women doubt themselves and then spend money to fix the doubt. It’s not neutral. And you can feel it.”

Charlotte stared at her for a beat, and some tension eased from her mouth.

Celeste’s tone stayed steady, but gentler now. “So we don’t do this like they want us to.”

Charlotte’s brow furrowed. “How do we do it.”

Celeste leaned in slightly, keeping it private. “We strip it back. We take control of the conditions.”

Charlotte’s eyes held hers.

“We leave,” Celeste said simply, “and we do it somewhere smaller. A boutique. One counter. One person. One scent at a time. Or we do samples at home.”

Charlotte exhaled, and the relief was immediate, like she’d been holding herself together with sheer force.

“At home,” Charlotte repeated, as if tasting the idea. “In a safe room.”

Celeste nodded. “With tea. With your own soap. Your own skin. Your own normal air.”

Charlotte’s shoulders dropped a fraction.

“And,” Celeste added, voice firm, “we name the intention again. We are not buying you a persona. We are choosing a tool you enjoy.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched faintly. “I forgot.”

Celeste’s eyes sharpened, not unkind. “You didn’t forget. You got overwhelmed.”

Charlotte looked at her, steadying. “I don’t like how quickly it happens.”

Celeste’s answer was immediate. “Then we treat it as data. We don’t shame it. We plan around it.”

Charlotte’s gaze warmed slightly, the way it did when she felt protected without being infantilised.

Celeste reached out and touched Charlotte’s hand—brief, grounding.

“One more thing,” Celeste said.

Charlotte nodded.

Celeste’s voice dropped. “If this is you trying to become ‘properly feminine’ by someone else’s definition, we stop. Today.”

Charlotte’s eyes held hers.

Then, very quietly, Charlotte said, “I don’t want their definition.”

Celeste’s chest eased.

“What do you want,” Celeste asked.

Charlotte looked down at the strip, then folded it in half and put it into her pocket like a note she’d keep.

“I want… my own,” Charlotte said. “And I want it to be quiet enough that I can still hear myself.”

Celeste felt her mouth curve—pride, affection, relief.

“Good,” Celeste said. “That’s the standard.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “We’re leaving.”

Celeste nodded. “We’re leaving.”

They turned together, not hurried, not flustered.

The saleswoman glanced up as they approached, ready to smooth, to sell, to rescue a sale.

Celeste smiled politely. “We’ll come back another day. Smaller dose.”

The woman nodded at once, professional and kind. “Of course. I can set aside a few samples if you like—clean musks, tea notes, soft woods.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to Celeste, asking without words: *is that safe?*

Celeste answered with a small nod: *we choose.*

“Yes,” Celeste said. “That would be helpful.”

They left the perfume floor and the air changed—less curated, less intrusive. Charlotte’s breathing loosened again, as if her body had been waiting for permission to exist without being managed by a marketing department.

Outside, on the street, Charlotte stopped and looked at Celeste with a seriousness that made Celeste’s chest tighten again.

“I’m sorry,” Charlotte said reflexively.

Celeste’s eyes sharpened. “Don’t.”

Charlotte blinked.

“You don’t apologise for noticing when something is wrong for you,” Celeste said. “That’s not weakness. That’s intelligence.”

Charlotte’s shoulders eased. “Okay.”

Celeste slipped her hand into Charlotte’s briefly—public, ordinary, untheatrical.

“Next time,” Celeste said, “we do it our way.”

Charlotte nodded once. “Our way.”

And they walked on, letting the city be the city—while Celeste quietly held the truth of it close:

Charlotte wasn’t fading.

She was learning the difference between femininity as a commodity and femininity as a life.

And she was choosing the latter.

**Robyn:**

I don't know if this is a consideration to document, but most young women have had the advantage in terms of life-experience of having been little girls. That is not to be under-estimated. I see it in my granddaughter - the constant reinforcement towards who she wants to be already, at 4. Charlie as Charlie had none of that. Well, very little. His mum taught him to sew, to be organised, to be tidy in his habits such as writing and arranging things, she allowed him to grow his hair as long as he wanted, and to respect women. Probably a few other things, but other than her being his sole role model thanks to an absentee father, he wasn't encouraged to be anything other than a boy. And he was fine with it, overall. He didn't enjoy it much, but he didn't give it much thought. He didn't have fantasies about being a superhero or a sports star... he was too much of a realist for that. And although he didn't want to be female, he thought body hair was monstrous, ugly, digusting. This was his personal fabric. So, for him to now be Charlotte isn't difficult: he's actually okay with it. But just as with masculine persona has demands, so does the feminine persona... just different, more internal, perhaps? Self aware? Perhaps that's where she needs to grow, now. Women look into eyes to understand the mind. They look at mirrors... something Charlotte needs to learn how to do. She needs to get comfortable looking at herself.

**Emily:**

That’s not only a consideration worth documenting — it’s one of the most *respectful* and psychologically true ways to frame Charlotte’s next stage without sliding into trope.

You’re naming something real: most women have had **a whole childhood of being socially mirrored as “girl”**—praised, corrected, coached, warned, protected, contradicted, dressed, photographed, compared. It builds an internal map: not always healthy, but *dense*. Charlotte didn’t get that density. She got fragments—your list is perfect: sewing, orderliness, hair permission, respect, a mother as primary model, and a private disgust for certain “male-coded” bodily realities without it turning into a declared wish to be female.

So Charlotte’s ease with “being Charlotte” makes sense. But ease is not the same as **embodiment**.

### The growth point you’ve identified is gold
> “Women look into eyes to understand the mind. They look at mirrors… something Charlotte needs to learn how to do.”

Yes. And notice how cleanly that fits your anti-fetish spine: it’s not about clothes, or passing, or attention. It’s about **self-recognition**.

Charlotte needs a “girlhood substitute” that isn’t infantilising:  
not lessons, not makeover sequences, not being “taught to be a woman,” but **a set of adult practices that build self-ownership**.

### A way to phrase it inside the story (so it doesn’t sound like an essay)
You can make this a Celeste insight: she realises Charlotte’s discomfort in the perfume hall wasn’t “perfume” — it was **a lack of internal scaffolding for self-regard**. And Celeste, being Celeste, doesn’t coo. She builds structure.

### The mirror problem: how to write it without trope
The trope version is “she stares in the mirror and cries.”  
Your version is:

- Charlotte avoids mirrors because mirrors used to be **evidence** (of wrongness, of scrutiny).
- Now mirrors need to become **tools** (of neutrality, of care, of calibration).
- Celeste reframes mirror time as *data collection*, not vanity.

And crucially: Charlotte retains agency. She isn’t “made” to do it.

### A scene seed you can drop in: “Two Minutes”
*(Celeste POV; short, functional, no melodrama)*

Celeste noticed it at home first, not in a dramatic way, in the way you noticed a missing habit.

Charlotte didn’t avoid mirrors exactly.

She just… never used them.

She brushed her hair without looking. Skipped past reflective surfaces like they were windows she didn’t need. Checked her teeth by feel, not sight. If her gaze landed on herself by accident, it slid away immediately, as if eye contact with her own reflection was impolite.

One evening, after dinner, Celeste washed a plate and watched Charlotte in the hallway.

Charlotte paused near the mirror by the door—paused long enough for Celeste to see the hesitation—then stepped around it.

Celeste dried her hands on a towel and said, quietly, “Charlotte.”

Charlotte stopped. Turned.

Celeste didn’t smile. Didn’t soften it. She spoke as if she were naming a policy.

“I want you to start looking at yourself,” Celeste said.

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “Why.”

“Because you keep disappearing,” Celeste replied. “Not from me. From you.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened—defensive, then thoughtful.

Celeste continued, calm. “You didn’t get a childhood of being mirrored as a girl. You didn’t get the boring repetition of being seen and learning to see yourself back.”

Charlotte stared at her.

Celeste added, “So we build the habit now. Not for beauty. For ownership.”

Charlotte’s throat worked. “I don’t like mirrors.”

“I know,” Celeste said. “That’s why we do it.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted. “You’re being strict.”

“Yes,” Celeste replied. “Because I’m not letting you outsource your selfhood to my eyes.”

Charlotte went still at that. The phrase landed.

Celeste pointed to the mirror by the door. “Two minutes. That’s it. Stand there. Look into your own eyes. No judgement. Just… contact.”

Charlotte’s voice came out small, annoyed. “That sounds ridiculous.”

Celeste didn’t blink. “It sounds necessary.”

Charlotte hesitated, then stepped in front of the mirror.

She didn’t pose. She didn’t adjust. She just stood.

At first she looked at her mouth, her hair, the line of her jaw—anything but her eyes.

Celeste said quietly, “Eyes.”

Charlotte lifted her gaze.

For a moment, she went very still—like she’d been caught doing something intimate in public.

And then—slowly—her expression changed.

Not joy. Not tears.

Recognition.

A kind of quiet settling, the same way a garment settled when it finally fit.

Celeste watched from the kitchen doorway, hands still, heart in her throat, and said nothing. Because this wasn’t hers to direct once it began.

After two minutes Celeste said, “Done.”

Charlotte exhaled as if she’d been holding her breath the whole time.

She didn’t look away with relief exactly. She looked away like someone finishing a hard exercise.

Celeste nodded once. “Again tomorrow.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “I hate you.”

Celeste’s lips curved. “No you don’t.”

Charlotte paused.

Then, very quietly, she said, “Thank you.”

Celeste’s chest tightened. “Good.”

And that was it. No performance. No ceremony. Just a new habit: Charlotte learning to meet herself without flinching.

### Why this works
- It acknowledges the “no girlhood” gap without pathologising it.
- It doesn’t make Charlotte childish.
- It doesn’t turn femininity into “pretty”; it turns it into **self-ownership**.
- It keeps Celeste powerful *and* wise: she refuses to be Charlotte’s mirror forever.

If you want, next we can write a companion beat from **Lauren’s POV** where she notices the same thing in a motherly way but chooses restraint (she doesn’t intrude), and it deepens the competence network: each woman supports Charlotte differently without taking her over.

**Robyn:**

The companion beat from Lauren’s POV where she notices the same thing in a motherly way but chooses restraint (she doesn’t intrude) is also an educational moment for Lauren. She intuitively understands the disadvantage in terms of childhood reinforcements Charlotte never had, which would now present a massive challenge from a personal acknowledgement perspective. If you can't even look at yourself in the mirror to see yourself clearly, how can you ever hope to be a comfortable, engaging, fulfilled woman: can't happen.

**Emily:**

## Companion Beat — “Restraint” (Lauren POV, past tense)

Lauren noticed it because Lauren noticed everything.

Not in a nosy way—she wasn’t that sort of mother—but in the way a woman who had spent years reading small signals could read a room without being told what was wrong.
